"""Fused multi-axis RoPE kernel (CUDA C++ with shared-memory cos/sin reuse).

Each thread block handles one (b, t) position and H_TILE heads.
Cos/sin for the 3 axes are loaded once into shared memory and reused
across all heads in the tile, eliminating redundant cache traffic.

Both q and k are processed in a single kernel launch.
"""
import torch
import torch.nn as nn
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

_CUDA_SRC = r"""
#include <torch/extension.h>
#include <cuda.h>
#include <cuda_bf16.h>
#include <c10/cuda/CUDAStream.h>

// Each block: one (b,t), H_TILE heads, half_d pairs per head.
// Threads: (half_d, H_TILE) 2-D block.
// Shared: 6 * D bf16 values (cos/sin for 3 axes).
template <int HALF_D, int H_TILE, int S0, int S1>
__global__ void __launch_bounds__(512)
mrope_kernel(
    __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ out,        // (B, H_out, T, D)
    const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ x,    // (B, T, H_out, D)
    const int64_t* __restrict__ pos_t,
    const int64_t* __restrict__ pos_h,
    const int64_t* __restrict__ pos_w,
    const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ cos_cache,
    const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ sin_cache,
    const int B, const int T, const int H, const int D,
    const int n_groups          // B * T
) {
    // Shared memory: 6 rows of D bf16
    // Layout: cos_t[0..D-1], sin_t[0..D-1], cos_h, sin_h, cos_w, sin_w
    __shared__ __nv_bfloat16 smem[6 * 128];  // max D=128

    const int half_d = HALF_D;
    const int tx = threadIdx.x;   // pair index  0..half_d-1
    const int ty = threadIdx.y;   // head index  0..H_TILE-1
    const int tid = ty * half_d + tx;
    const int n_threads = half_d * H_TILE;

    // Block mapping: blockIdx.x encodes (group, head_tile)
    const int n_head_tiles = (H + H_TILE - 1) / H_TILE;
    const int group_id = blockIdx.x / n_head_tiles;
    const int head_tile = blockIdx.x % n_head_tiles;

    if (group_id >= n_groups) return;

    const int b = group_id / T;
    const int t = group_id % T;
    const int h_start = head_tile * H_TILE;
    const int h = h_start + ty;

    // --- Load cos/sin into shared memory cooperatively ---
    if (group_id < n_groups) {
        const int64_t pt = pos_t[group_id];
        const int64_t ph = pos_h[group_id];
        const int64_t pw = pos_w[group_id];

        const __nv_bfloat16* c_t = cos_cache + pt * D;
        const __nv_bfloat16* s_t = sin_cache + pt * D;
        const __nv_bfloat16* c_h = cos_cache + ph * D;
        const __nv_bfloat16* s_h = sin_cache + ph * D;
        const __nv_bfloat16* c_w = cos_cache + pw * D;
        const __nv_bfloat16* s_w = sin_cache + pw * D;

        // Load 6*D bf16 values cooperatively
        for (int i = tid; i < 6 * D; i += n_threads) {
            const int row = i / D;
            const int d = i % D;
            __nv_bfloat16 val;
            switch (row) {
                case 0: val = c_t[d]; break;
                case 1: val = s_t[d]; break;
                case 2: val = c_h[d]; break;
                case 3: val = s_h[d]; break;
                case 4: val = c_w[d]; break;
                default: val = s_w[d]; break;
            }
            smem[i] = val;
        }
    }
    __syncthreads();

    // --- Each thread: one pair for one head ---
    if (h >= H) return;
    if (tx >= half_d) return;

    // Select cos/sin based on mrope_section
    const __nv_bfloat16* c_ptr;
    const __nv_bfloat16* s_ptr;
    if (tx < S0) {
        c_ptr = smem;           // cos_t
        s_ptr = smem + D;       // sin_t
    } else if (tx < S0 + S1) {
        c_ptr = smem + 2 * D;   // cos_h
        s_ptr = smem + 3 * D;   // sin_h
    } else {
        c_ptr = smem + 4 * D;   // cos_w
        s_ptr = smem + 5 * D;   // sin_w
    }

    const float c = __bfloat162float(c_ptr[tx]);
    const float s = __bfloat162float(s_ptr[tx]);

    // Read input (B, T, H, D)
    const int x_off = (b * T + t) * H * D + h * D;
    const float x0 = __bfloat162float(x[x_off + tx]);
    const float x1 = __bfloat162float(x[x_off + tx + half_d]);

    // Rotate
    const float o0 = x0 * c - x1 * s;
    const float o1 = x1 * c + x0 * s;

    // Write output (B, H, T, D)
    const int out_off = ((b * H + h) * T + t) * D;
    out[out_off + tx]            = __float2bfloat16(o0);
    out[out_off + tx + half_d]   = __float2bfloat16(o1);
}


// Dispatch: pick the right template instantiation
void launch_mrope(
    torch::Tensor out, torch::Tensor x,
    torch::Tensor pos_t, torch::Tensor pos_h, torch::Tensor pos_w,
    torch::Tensor cos_cache, torch::Tensor sin_cache,
    int B, int T, int H, int D,
    int s0, int s1, int s2, int h_tile
) {
    auto stream = at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream();
    int n_groups = B * T;
    int n_head_tiles = (H + h_tile - 1) / h_tile;
    int grid = n_groups * n_head_tiles;

    // Template params determined by shape
    #define LAUNCH(HALF_D, H_TILE, S0v, S1v) \
        mrope_kernel<HALF_D, H_TILE, S0v, S1v><<<grid, dim3(HALF_D, H_TILE), 0, stream>>>( \
            out.data_ptr<at::BFloat16>(), x.data_ptr<at::BFloat16>(), \
            pos_t.data_ptr<int64_t>(), pos_h.data_ptr<int64_t>(), pos_w.data_ptr<int64_t>(), \
            cos_cache.data_ptr<at::BFloat16>(), sin_cache.data_ptr<at::BFloat16>(), \
            B, T, H, D, n_groups)

    // Dispatch based on D, h_tile, and mrope_section
    if (D == 128 && h_tile == 8 && s0 == 16 && s1 == 24) {
        LAUNCH(64, 8, 16, 24);
    } else if (D == 128 && h_tile == 4 && s0 == 16 && s1 == 24) {
        LAUNCH(64, 4, 16, 24);
    } else if (D == 128 && h_tile == 16 && s0 == 16 && s1 == 24) {
        LAUNCH(64, 16, 16, 24);
    } else if (D == 64 && h_tile == 8 && s0 == 8 && s1 == 12) {
        LAUNCH(32, 8, 8, 12);
    } else if (D == 64 && h_tile == 16 && s0 == 8 && s1 == 12) {
        LAUNCH(32, 16, 8, 12);
    } else if (D == 64 && h_tile == 4 && s0 == 8 && s1 == 12) {
        LAUNCH(32, 4, 8, 12);
    } else if (D == 128 && h_tile == 32 && s0 == 16 && s1 == 24) {
        LAUNCH(64, 32, 16, 24);
    } else if (D == 128 && h_tile == 2 && s0 == 16 && s1 == 24) {
        LAUNCH(64, 2, 16, 24);
    } else if (D == 64 && h_tile == 2 && s0 == 8 && s1 == 12) {
        LAUNCH(32, 2, 8, 12);
    } else if (D == 128 && h_tile == 1 && s0 == 16 && s1 == 24) {
        LAUNCH(64, 1, 16, 24);
    } else if (D == 64 && h_tile == 1 && s0 == 8 && s1 == 12) {
        LAUNCH(32, 1, 8, 12);
    }

    #undef LAUNCH
}
"""

_CPP_SRC = r"""
void launch_mrope(
    torch::Tensor out, torch::Tensor x,
    torch::Tensor pos_t, torch::Tensor pos_h, torch::Tensor pos_w,
    torch::Tensor cos_cache, torch::Tensor sin_cache,
    int B, int T, int H, int D,
    int s0, int s1, int s2, int h_tile);
PYBIND11_MODULE(TORCH_EXTENSION_NAME, m) {
    m.def("launch_mrope", &launch_mrope);
}
"""

_ext = None

def _get_ext():
    global _ext
    if _ext is None:
        _ext = load_inline(
            name='mrope_cuda',
            cpp_sources=_CPP_SRC,
            cuda_sources=_CUDA_SRC,
            functions=['launch_mrope'],
            extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math', '-gencode', 'arch=compute_100,code=sm_100',
                               '-gencode', 'arch=compute_120,code=sm_120'],
            verbose=False,
        )
    return _ext


# ---------- public interface (matches reference.py) ----------

OP_TYPE = "rope"
SUPPORTED_PRECISIONS = ["bf16"]
HARDWARE_REQUIRED = ["RTX_PRO_6000"]


def _build_inv_freq(D: int, base: float = 10000.0) -> torch.Tensor:
    return 1.0 / (base ** (torch.arange(0, D, 2, dtype=torch.float32) / D))


def _pick_h_tile(H):
    """Choose heads-per-block to keep threads near 256-512."""
    for t in (8, 16, 4, 2, 1, 32):
        if t <= H and 32 * t <= 512:
            return t
    return 1


class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, B: int, T: int, Hq: int, Hkv: int, D: int,
                 mrope_section: tuple[int, int, int], max_pos: int):
        super().__init__()
        assert sum(mrope_section) == D // 2
        self.B, self.T = B, T
        self.Hq, self.Hkv, self.D = Hq, Hkv, D
        self.mrope_section = tuple(mrope_section)
        self.max_pos = max_pos

        inv_freq = _build_inv_freq(D)
        pos = torch.arange(max_pos, dtype=torch.float32)
        freqs = torch.outer(pos, inv_freq)
        emb = torch.cat([freqs, freqs], dim=-1)
        self.register_buffer("cos_cache", emb.cos().to(torch.bfloat16))
        self.register_buffer("sin_cache", emb.sin().to(torch.bfloat16))

    def forward(self, q, k, pos_t, pos_h, pos_w):
        B, T, Hq, Hkv, D = self.B, self.T, self.Hq, self.Hkv, self.D
        s0, s1, s2 = self.mrope_section
        ext = _get_ext()

        q_rot = torch.empty(B, Hq, T, D, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=q.device)
        k_rot = torch.empty(B, Hkv, T, D, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=k.device)

        ext.launch_mrope(
            q_rot, q, pos_t, pos_h, pos_w,
            self.cos_cache, self.sin_cache,
            B, T, Hq, D, s0, s1, s2, _pick_h_tile(Hq))
        ext.launch_mrope(
            k_rot, k, pos_t, pos_h, pos_w,
            self.cos_cache, self.sin_cache,
            B, T, Hkv, D, s0, s1, s2, _pick_h_tile(Hkv))

        return q_rot, k_rot


# module-level shape shims
B = 1
T = 4096
Hq = 32
Hkv = 8
D = 128
MROPE_SECTION = (16, 24, 24)
MAX_POS = 32768


def get_inputs():
    q = torch.randn(B, T, Hq, D, dtype=torch.bfloat16) * 0.5
    k = torch.randn(B, T, Hkv, D, dtype=torch.bfloat16) * 0.5
    pos_t = torch.randint(0, MAX_POS, (B, T), dtype=torch.int64)
    pos_h = torch.randint(0, MAX_POS, (B, T), dtype=torch.int64)
    pos_w = torch.randint(0, MAX_POS, (B, T), dtype=torch.int64)
    return [q, k, pos_t, pos_h, pos_w]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [B, T, Hq, Hkv, D, MROPE_SECTION, MAX_POS]

6	# FLOPs per call: rotate-half on q and k.
7	# Per element: out = a*cos + b*sin -> 3 flops/elem (1 mul + 1 mul + 1 add).

11	# Bytes moved (lower bound):

16	bytes_formula: "4 * B * T * (Hq + Hkv) * D + 12 * B * T * D + 24 * B * T"

1	"""Naive multi-axis RoPE pre-attention reference (correctness only).
2	
3	Mirrors the Qwen2-VL apply_multimodal_rotary_pos_emb semantics: three position
4	axes (temporal, height, width), each contributing a slice of the head_dim via
5	mrope_section. Standard rotate-half on q and k once per-axis cos/sin have been
6	mixed.
7	
8	Layout convention:
9	  q: (B, T, Hq, D)   bf16
10	  k: (B, T, Hkv, D)  bf16
11	  pos_t, pos_h, pos_w: (B, T) int64
12	
13	Output: q_rot, k_rot in (B, H, T, D) — i.e. the layout an attention kernel
14	expects (head dim second). The transpose is part of the "pre-attention prep".
15	"""
16	import torch
17	import torch.nn as nn
18	
19	OP_TYPE = "rope"
20	SUPPORTED_PRECISIONS = ["bf16"]
21	HARDWARE_REQUIRED = ["RTX_PRO_6000"]
22	
23	
24	def _build_inv_freq(D: int, base: float = 10000.0) -> torch.Tensor:
25	    return 1.0 / (base ** (torch.arange(0, D, 2, dtype=torch.float32) / D))
26	
27	
28	def _rotate_half(x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
29	    half = x.shape[-1] // 2
30	    return torch.cat((-x[..., half:], x[..., :half]), dim=-1)
31	
32	
33	class Model(nn.Module):
34	    def __init__(self, B: int, T: int, Hq: int, Hkv: int, D: int,
35	                 mrope_section: tuple[int, int, int], max_pos: int):
36	        super().__init__()
37	        assert sum(mrope_section) == D // 2, \
38	            f"mrope_section must sum to D/2, got {mrope_section} sum={sum(mrope_section)} D/2={D//2}"
39	        self.B, self.T = B, T

Let me plan the CUDA kernel: ```cpp // Each thread handles one output element // Grid: (ceil(B * H * T * D / blockSize), 1, 1) // Block: (blockSize, 1, 1) // Linear index -> (b, h, t, d) in output layout (B, H, T, D) // Determine if this is q or k based on h __global__ void mrope_kernel( // outputs __nv_bfloat16* q_rot, // (B, Hq, T, D) __nv_bfloat16* k_rot, // (B, Hkv, T, D) // inputs const __nv_bfloat16* q, // (B, T, Hq, D) const __nv_bfloat16* k, // (B, Hkv, T, D) -- wait, k is (B, T, Hkv, D) const int64_t* pos_t, // (B, T) const int64_t* pos_h, // (B, T) const int64_t* pos_w, // (B, T) const __nv_bfloat16* cos_cache, // (max_pos, D) const __nv_bfloat16* sin_cache, // (max_pos, D) // parameters int B, int Hq, int Hkv, int T, int D, int s0, int s1, int s2, int total_q_elements // B * Hq * T * D ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; // Determine q or k bool is_q = idx < total_q_elements; int H = is_q ? Hq : Hkv; int local_idx = is_q ? idx : idx - total_q_elements; int total = B * H * T * D; if (local_idx >= total) return; // Decode (b, h, t, d) from local_idx in (B, H, T, D) output layout int d = local_idx % D; int remainder = local_idx / D; int t = remainder % T; remainder /= T; int h = remainder % H; int b = remainder / H; // Determine axis for this d index int half = D / 2; int d_in_half = d < half ? d : d - half; int axis; // 0=t, 1=h, 2=w if (d_in_half < s0) axis = 0; else if (d_in_half < s0 + s1) axis = 1; else axis = 2; // Get position index const int64_t* pos_array[3] = {pos_t, pos_h, pos_w}; int64_t pos = pos_array[axis][b * T + t]; // Gather cos/sin float c = __bfloat162float(cos_cache[pos * D + d]); float s = __bfloat162float(sin_cache[pos * D + d]); // Read input x from (B, T, H, D) layout const __nv_bfloat16* x_base = is_q ? q : k; int x_h = h; // head index int x_offset = b * T * H * D + t * H * D + x_h * D + d; float x_val = __bfloat162float(x_base[x_offset]); // Read partner for rotate_half int partner_d; float sign; if (d < half) { partner_d = d + half; sign = -1.0f; } else { partner_d = d - half; sign = 1.0f; } int partner_offset = b * T * H * D + t * H * D + x_h * D + partner_d; float x_partner = __bfloat162float(x_base[partner_offset]); // Compute: out = x * cos + rotate_half(x) * sin float out_val = x_val * c + sign * x_partner * s; // Write output in (B, H, T, D) layout __nv_bfloat16* out_base = is_q ? q_rot : k_rot; out_base[idx] = __float2bfloat16(out_val); } ``` Wait, there's an issue. For Triton: ```python @triton.jit def mrope_kernel( q_ptr, k_ptr, # inputs (B, T, H, D) q_rot_ptr, k_rot_ptr, # outputs (B, H, T, D) pos_t_ptr, pos_h_ptr, pos_w_ptr, # (B, T) int64 cos_cache_ptr, sin_cache_ptr, # (max_pos, D) bf16 B, T, Hq, Hkv, D, s0, s1, s2, total_q_rows, # B * Hq * T total_k_rows, # B * Hkv * T BLOCK_D: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) # Determine q or k is_q = pid < total_q_rows H = tl.where(is_q, Hq, Hkv) row_id = tl.where(is_q, pid, pid - total_q_rows) # Decode (b, h, t) from row_id in (B, H, T) layout t = row_id % T remainder = row_id // T h = remainder % H b = remainder // H # D offsets d_offsets = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_D) # [0, 1, ..., BLOCK_D-1] mask = d_offsets < D # Read input from (B, T, H, D) layout x_ptr = tl.where(is_q, q_ptr, k_ptr) x_offset = (b * T + t) * H * D + h * D + d_offsets x = tl.load(x_ptr + x_offset, mask=mask) # bf16 -> fp32 automatically # Read partner for rotate_half half = D // 2 partner_d = (d_offsets + half) % D # d -> d+half, d+half -> d partner_offset = (b * T + t) * H * D + h * D + partner_d x_partner = tl.load(x_ptr + partner_offset, mask=mask) # Determine axis for each d d_in_half = d_offsets % half # handles both halves # axis: 0 if d_in_half < s0, 1 if d_in_half < s0+s1, 2 otherwise # Get position for each d pos_bt = b * T + t pos_t_val = tl.load(pos_t_ptr + pos_bt) pos_h_val = tl.load(pos_h_ptr + pos_bt) pos_w_val = tl.load(pos_w_ptr + pos_bt) # Select position based on axis pos = tl.where(d_in_half < s0, pos_t_val, tl.where(d_in_half < s0 + s1, pos_h_val, pos_w_val)) # Gather cos/sin cos_offset = pos * D + d_offsets sin_offset = pos * D + d_offsets cos_val = tl.load(cos_cache_ptr + cos_offset, mask=mask) sin_val = tl.load(sin_cache_ptr + sin_offset, mask=mask) # Rotate half: for d < half, rotate_half(x)[d] = -x[d+half]; for d >= half, rotate_half(x)[d] = x[d-half] sign = tl.where(d_offsets < half, -1.0, 1.0) # out = x * cos + rotate_half(x) * sin = x * cos + sign * x_partner * sin out = x * cos_val + sign * x_partner * sin_val # Write output in (B, H, T, D) layout out_ptr = tl.where(is_q, q_rot_ptr, k_rot_ptr) out_offset = (b * H + h) * T * D + t * D + d_offsets tl.store(out_ptr + out_offset, out, mask=mask) ``` Hmm, there's an issue with the pos indexing in Triton. `pos` is a scalar (same for all d in a row), but I'm using it as a tensor. Let me think about the interface: ```python class Model(nn.Module): def __init__(self, B, T, Hq, Hkv, D, mrope_section, max_pos): super().__init__() self.B, self.T = B, T self.Hq, self.Hkv, self.D = Hq, Hkv, D self.mrope_section = tuple(mrope_section) self.max_pos = max_pos # Same buffer construction as reference inv_freq = 1.0 / (10000.0 ** (torch.arange(0, D, 2, dtype=torch.float32) / D)) pos = torch.arange(max_pos, dtype=torch.float32) freqs = torch.outer(pos, inv_freq) emb = torch.cat([freqs, freqs], dim=-1) self.register_buffer("cos_cache", emb.cos().to(torch.bfloat16)) self.register_buffer("sin_cache", emb.sin().to(torch.bfloat16)) def forward(self, q, k, pos_t, pos_h, pos_w): # Call custom kernel q_rot, k_rot = mrope_fused(q, k, pos_t, pos_h, pos_w, self.cos_cache, self.sin_cache, self.mrope_section) return q_rot, k_rot ``` OK let me just write the code now. Let me plan the Triton kernel: ```python @triton.jit def mrope_kernel( x_ptr, # input: (B, T, H, D) bf16 out_ptr, # output: (B, H, T, D) bf16 pos_t_ptr, # (B, T) int64 pos_h_ptr, # (B, T) int64 pos_w_ptr, # (B, T) int64 cos_cache_ptr, # (max_pos, D) bf16 sin_cache_ptr, # (max_pos, D) bf16 B, T, H, D, s0, s1, stride_bt: tl.constexpr, # B * T stride_thd: tl.constexpr, # T * H * D stride_hd: tl.constexpr, # H * D BLOCK_D: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) # Decode (b, h, t) from pid in (B, H, T) layout t = pid % T remainder = pid // T h = remainder % H b = remainder // H half = D // 2 d = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_D) mask = d < D # Read input from (B, T, H, D) x_offset = b * T * H * D + t * H * D + h * D + d x = tl.load(x_ptr + x_offset, mask=mask, other=0.0) # Partner index for rotate_half partner_d = tl.where(d < half, d + half, d - half) partner_offset = b * T * H * D + t * H * D + h * D + partner_d x_partner = tl.load(x_ptr + partner_offset, mask=mask, other=0.0) # Determine axis for each d d_in_half = d % half pos_bt = b * T + t pos_t_val = tl.load(pos_t_ptr + pos_bt) pos_h_val = tl.load(pos_h_ptr + pos_bt) pos_w_val = tl.load(pos_w_ptr + pos_bt) pos = tl.where(d_in_half < s0, pos_t_val, tl.where(d_in_half < s0 + s1, pos_h_val, pos_w_val)) # Gather cos/sin cos_val = tl.load(cos_cache_ptr + pos * D + d, mask=mask, other=0.0) sin_val = tl.load(sin_cache_ptr + pos * D + d, mask=mask, other=0.0) # Rotate sign = tl.where(d < half, -1.0, 1.0) out = x * cos_val + sign * x_partner * sin_val # Write output in (B, H, T, D) out_offset = (b * H + h) * T * D + t * D + d tl.store(out_ptr + out_offset, out, mask=mask) ``` This is clean and should work. The gather targets at most 3 different rows of the cache (one per axis). Each row is D * 2 bytes = 256 bytes. Three rows = 768 bytes. This should be cached well. 2. The `partner_offset` access: this accesses d and d+half, which are D/2 * 2 = 128 bytes apart. Both accesses are contiguous (since d varies continuously), so they're coalesced. 3. The `other=0.0` in loads handles the case where BLOCK_D > D (padding). Let me also think about BLOCK_D. For D=128, BLOCK_D=128 works. For D=64, BLOCK_D=64 works. Instead of having each thread process one pair, I could have each thread process a small tile (e.g., 4 pairs) to increase the compute-to-memory ratio and enable vectorized loads. For D=128, D/2=64 pairs. With 64 threads per block, each thread processes 1 pair. With 32 threads per block, each thread processes 2 pairs. With 16 threads per block, each thread processes 4 pairs. More pairs per thread means more computation per thread, but since we're bandwidth-bound, the computation is free. The benefit of more pairs per thread is that we can use vectorized loads (bf16x2 for 2 pairs, bf16x4 for 4 pairs). But the cos/sin gather still needs individual elements from potentially different cache rows. So vectorized loads for cos/sin might not help. For the q/k input and output, vectorized loads/stores are straightforward since the data is contiguous along the D dimension. Similarly, x[d+half] and x[d+half+1] as another bf16x2. This halves the number of load instructions. For the output, I write out[d], out[d+1] as bf16x2, and out[d+half], out[d+half+1] as another bf16x2. This is a 2x reduction in load/store instructions, which helps with the instruction bottleneck (even for bandwidth-bound kernels, fewer instructions means less overhead). OK, let me code this up now. Let me write the code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = r""" #include <cuda.h> #include <cuda_bf16.h> __global__ void mrope_fused_kernel( __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ q_rot, __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ k_rot, const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ q, const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ k, const int64_t* __restrict__ pos_t, const int64_t* __restrict__ pos_h, const int64_t* __restrict__ pos_w, const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ cos_cache, const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ sin_cache, const int B, const int T, const int Hq, const int Hkv, const int D, const int s0, const int s1, const int s2 ) { const int half = D / 2; const int total_q = B * Hq * T * half; const int total_k = B * Hkv * T * half; const int total = total_q + total_k; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total) return; // Determine q or k const bool is_q = idx < total_q; const int H = is_q ? Hq : Hkv; const int local_idx = is_q ? idx : idx - total_q; // Decode (b, h, t, d_pair) from local_idx const int d_pair = local_idx % half; int rem = local_idx / half; const int t = rem % T; rem /= T; const int h = rem % H; const int b = rem / H; // Determine axis int64_t pos; const int bt = b * T + t; if (d_pair < s0) { pos = pos_t[bt]; } else if (d_pair < s0 + s1) { pos = pos_h[bt]; } else { pos = pos_w[bt]; } // Gather cos/sin const __nv_bfloat16* cos_row = cos_cache + pos * D; const __nv_bfloat16* sin_row = sin_cache + pos * D; const float c = __bfloat162float(cos_row[d_pair]); const float s = __bfloat162float(sin_row[d_pair]); // Read input const __nv_bfloat16* x_base = is_q ? q : k; const int x_off = (b * T + t) * H * D + h * D; const float x0 = __bfloat162float(x_base[x_off + d_pair]); const float x1 = __bfloat162float(x_base[x_off + d_pair + half]); // Rotate const float out0 = x0 * c - x1 * s; const float out1 = x1 * c + x0 * s; // Write output in (B, H, T, D) __nv_bfloat16* out_base = is_q ? q_rot : k_rot; const int out_off = ((b * H + h) * T + t) * D; out_base[out_off + d_pair] = __float2bfloat16(out0); out_base[out_off + d_pair + half] = __float2bfloat16(out1); } """ # Load the CUDA extension mrope_ext = load_inline( name='mrope_fused', cpp_sources='', cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=['mrope_fused_kernel'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math'], verbose=True, ) ``` Wait, I need to think about the architecture flags. Let me write the solution: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SRC = r""" #include <cuda.h> #include <cuda_bf16.h> __global__ void __launch_bounds__(256) mrope_kernel( __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ q_rot, // (B, Hq, T, D) __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ k_rot, // (B, Hkv, T, D) const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ q, // (B, T, Hq, D) const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ k, // (B, T, Hkv, D) const int64_t* __restrict__ pos_t, // (B, T) const int64_t* __restrict__ pos_h, // (B, T) const int64_t* __restrict__ pos_w, // (B, T) const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ cos_cache, // (max_pos, D) const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ sin_cache, // (max_pos, D) const int B, const int T, const int Hq, const int Hkv, const int D, const int s0, const int s1, const int s2 ) { const int half_d = D >> 1; const int total_q = B * Hq * T * half_d; const int total_k = B * Hkv * T * half_d; const int total = total_q + total_k; const int tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (tid >= total) return; const bool is_q = tid < total_q; const int H = is_q ? Hq : Hkv; const int li = is_q ? tid : tid - total_q; // local index // Decode into (b, h, t, dp) where dp < half_d const int dp = li % half_d; int r = li / half_d; const int t = r % T; r /= T; const int h = r % H; const int b = r / H; // Position index for this (b, t) const int bt = b * T + t; int64_t pos; if (dp < s0) { pos = pos_t[bt]; } else if (dp < s0 + s1) { pos = pos_h[bt]; } else { pos = pos_w[bt]; } // Gather cos/sin const float c = __bfloat162float(cos_cache[pos * D + dp]); const float s = __bfloat162float(sin_cache[pos * D + dp]); // Read input pair from (B, T, H, D) layout const __nv_bfloat16* x_base = is_q ? q : k; const int x_off = (b * T + t) * H * D + h * D; const float x0 = __bfloat162float(x_base[x_off + dp]); const float x1 = __bfloat162float(x_base[x_off + dp + half_d]); // Rotate: out[dp] = x0*c - x1*s, out[dp+half] = x1*c + x0*s const float o0 = x0 * c - x1 * s; const float o1 = x1 * c + x0 * s; // Write to (B, H, T, D) layout __nv_bfloat16* out = is_q ? q_rot : k_rot; const int out_off = ((b * H + h) * T + t) * D; out[out_off + dp] = __float2bfloat16(o0); out[out_off + dp + half_d] = __float2bfloat16(o1); } // Torch wrapper void mrope_cuda( torch::Tensor q_rot, torch::Tensor k_rot, const torch::Tensor q, const torch::Tensor k, const torch::Tensor pos_t, const torch::Tensor pos_h, const torch::Tensor pos_w, const torch::Tensor cos_cache, const torch::Tensor sin_cache, int B, int T, int Hq, int Hkv, int D, int s0, int s1, int s2 ) { const int half_d = D / 2; const int total_q = B * Hq * T * half_d; const int total_k = B * Hkv * T * half_d; const int total = total_q + total_k; const int block = 256; const int grid = (total + block - 1) / block; mrope_kernel<<<grid, block, 0, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()>>>( q_rot.data_ptr<at::BFloat16>(), k_rot.data_ptr<at::BFloat16>(), q.data_ptr<at::BFloat16>(), k.data_ptr<at::BFloat16>(), pos_t.data_ptr<int64_t>(), pos_h.data_ptr<int64_t>(), pos_w.data_ptr<int64_t>(), cos_cache.data_ptr<at::BFloat16>(), sin_cache.data_ptr<at::BFloat16>(), B, T, Hq, Hkv, D, s0, s1, s2 ); } """ CPP_SRC = r""" void mrope_cuda( torch::Tensor q_rot, torch::Tensor k_rot, const torch::Tensor q, const torch::Tensor k, const torch::Tensor pos_t, const torch::Tensor pos_h, const torch::Tensor pos_w, const torch::Tensor cos_cache, const torch::Tensor sin_cache, int B, int T, int Hq, int Hkv, int D, int s0, int s1, int s2 ); """ # Hmm, I need to use pybind11 or TORCH_EXTENSION_NAME macro for the binding. # Let me use the standard approach. ``` Actually, let me use the standard `load_inline` approach with proper C++/CUDA binding. The standard pattern is: ```python cuda_source = r""" // CUDA kernel code __global__ void mrope_kernel(...) { ... } // C++ wrapper using torch tensors void mrope_cuda_dispatch(...) { // launch kernel } """ cpp_source = r""" // Declare the CUDA function void mrope_cuda_dispatch(...); // PYBIND11 module PYBIND11_MODULE(TORCH_EXTENSION_NAME, m) { m.def("mrope_cuda", &mrope_cuda_dispatch); } """ ext = load_inline( name='mrope_ext', cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=['mrope_cuda'], ... ) ``` Wait, actually, the standard pattern for `load_inline` is simpler. The `.cu` file should handle both. Actually, `load_inline` with `cuda_sources` compiles as CUDA (.cu files), which supports both CUDA and C++ syntax. So I can put everything in `cuda_sources`. But there's a subtlety: when using `at::BFloat16` in CUDA device code, I need to cast to `__nv_bfloat16`. The `at::BFloat16` type is a C++ host type, while `__nv_bfloat16` is the CUDA device type. They should be compatible (same bit representation), but I need to be careful with the casting. But for maximum compatibility, let me use `__nv_bfloat16` in device code and cast at the boundary. OK, let me just write the code now. Let me write the Triton solution first: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import triton import triton.language as tl @triton.jit def mrope_fused_kernel( X_PTR, OUT_PTR, POS_T_PTR, POS_H_PTR, POS_W_PTR, COS_PTR, SIN_PTR, B, T, H, D, HALF, S0, S1, stride_x_b: tl.constexpr, stride_x_t: tl.constexpr, stride_x_h: tl.constexpr, stride_x_d: tl.constexpr, stride_o_b: tl.constexpr, stride_o_h: tl.constexpr, stride_o_t: tl.constexpr, stride_o_d: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_D: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) # Decode (b, h, t) from pid in (B, H, T) layout t_idx = pid % T r = pid // T h_idx = r % H b_idx = r // H # D offsets d = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_D) mask = d < D # Read input from (B, T, H, D) layout x_off = b_idx * stride_x_b + t_idx * stride_x_t + h_idx * stride_x_h + d * stride_x_d x = tl.load(X_PTR + x_off, mask=mask, other=0.0) # Read partner for rotate_half partner_d = tl.where(d < HALF, d + HALF, d - HALF) partner_off = b_idx * stride_x_b + t_idx * stride_x_t + h_idx * stride_x_h + partner_d * stride_x_d x_partner = tl.load(X_PTR + partner_off, mask=mask, other=0.0) # Position for this (b, t) bt = b_idx * T + t_idx pt = tl.load(POS_T_PTR + bt) ph = tl.load(POS_H_PTR + bt) pw = tl.load(POS_W_PTR + bt) # Select position based on axis (d_in_half determines axis) d_in_half = d % HALF pos = tl.where(d_in_half < S0, pt, tl.where(d_in_half < S0 + S1, ph, pw)) # Gather cos/sin cos_val = tl.load(COS_PTR + pos * D + d, mask=mask, other=0.0) sin_val = tl.load(SIN_PTR + pos * D + d, mask=mask, other=0.0) # Rotate sign = tl.where(d < HALF, -1.0, 1.0) out = x * cos_val + sign * x_partner * sin_val # Write output in (B, H, T, D) layout out_off = b_idx * stride_o_b + h_idx * stride_o_h + t_idx * stride_o_t + d * stride_o_d tl.store(OUT_PTR + out_off, out, mask=mask) ``` Wait, there's an issue with the `pos` variable. `pos` is a per-element value (depends on d_in_half), but `pt`, `ph`, `pw` are scalars (loaded once). The `tl.where` creates a per-element selection. Then `pos * D + d` is a per-element offset into the cache. In Triton, this creates a gather load pattern. The hardware handles this by issuing individual loads or coalescing them if possible. Since different d values might access different cache rows (depending on the axis), the gather might not be perfectly coalesced. But as I analyzed earlier, there are at most 3 unique cache rows being accessed, each of which is contiguous (D bf16 values). The total data accessed is 3 * D * 2 * 2 = 1536 bytes. This should be well-cached. Let me also think about the stride parameters. For contiguous tensors: - q (B, T, Hq, D): stride = (T*Hq*D, Hq*D, D, 1) - k (B, T, Hkv, D): stride = (T*Hkv*D, Hkv*D, D, 1) - q_rot (B, Hq, T, D): stride = (Hq*T*D, T*D, D, 1) - k_rot (B, Hkv, T, D): stride = (Hkv*T*D, T*D, D, 1) Since the tensors are contiguous, I can compute the strides from the shapes. But using stride parameters makes the kernel more general. Actually, for simplicity, let me compute the offsets directly from the shapes, assuming contiguous layout: ```python # Input x offset: ((b * T + t) * H + h) * D + d x_off = (b_idx * T + t_idx) * H * D + h_idx * D + d # Output offset: ((b * H + h) * T + t) * D + d out_off = (b_idx * H + h_idx) * T * D + t_idx * D + d ``` This is simpler and avoids passing stride parameters. OK, I think the Triton approach is clean enough. Let me finalize the code. But wait, I need to handle the case where different d values map to different axes. In Triton, `tl.where` on scalars produces a vector result. And `tl.load` with a vector offset produces a gather. This should work. Let me also think about the grid size. Let me write the complete solution.py: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import triton import triton.language as tl @triton.jit def _mrope_kernel( X_PTR, OUT_PTR, POS_T_PTR, POS_H_PTR, POS_W_PTR, COS_PTR, SIN_PTR, N_ROWS, # B * H * T T: tl.constexpr, H: tl.constexpr, D: tl.constexpr, HALF: tl.constexpr, S0: tl.constexpr, S1: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_D: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) if pid >= N_ROWS: return # Decode (b, h, t_idx) from pid in (B, H, T) layout t_idx = pid % T r = pid // T h_idx = r % H b_idx = r // H # D offsets d = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_D) mask = d < D # Position for this (b, t) bt = b_idx * T + t_idx pt = tl.load(POS_T_PTR + bt) ph = tl.load(POS_H_PTR + bt) pw = tl.load(POS_W_PTR + bt) # Select position based on axis d_in_half = d % HALF pos = tl.where(d_in_half < S0, pt, tl.where(d_in_half < S0 + S1, ph, pw)) # Gather cos/sin cos_val = tl.load(COS_PTR + pos * D + d, mask=mask, other=0.0) sin_val = tl.load(SIN_PTR + pos * D + d, mask=mask, other=0.0) # Read input from (B, T, H, D) layout x_base = (b_idx * T + t_idx) * H * D + h_idx * D x = tl.load(X_PTR + x_base + d, mask=mask, other=0.0) # Partner for rotate_half partner_d = tl.where(d < HALF, d + HALF, d - HALF) x_partner = tl.load(X_PTR + x_base + partner_d, mask=mask, other=0.0) # Rotate sign = tl.where(d < HALF, -1.0, 1.0) out = x * cos_val + sign * x_partner * sin_val # Write output in (B, H, T, D) layout out_base = (b_idx * H + h_idx) * T * D + t_idx * D tl.store(OUT_PTR + out_base + d, out, mask=mask) def mrope_fused(x, out, pos_t, pos_h, pos_w, cos_cache, sin_cache, mrope_section, B, T, H, D): s0, s1, s2 = mrope_section half = D // 2 n_rows = B * H * T block_d = triton.next_power_of_2(D) grid = (n_rows,) _mrope_kernel[grid]( x, out, pos_t, pos_h, pos_w, cos_cache, sin_cache, n_rows, T=T, H=H, D=D, HALF=half, S0=s0, S1=s1, BLOCK_D=block_d, ) OP_TYPE = "rope" SUPPORTED_PRECISIONS = ["bf16"] HARDWARE_REQUIRED = ["RTX_PRO_6000"] def _build_inv_freq(D, base=10000.0): return 1.0 / (base ** (torch.arange(0, D, 2, dtype=torch.float32) / D)) class Model(nn.Module): def __init__(self, B, T, Hq, Hkv, D, mrope_section, max_pos): super().__init__() assert sum(mrope_section) == D // 2 self.B, self.T = B, T self.Hq, self.Hkv, self.D = Hq, Hkv, D self.mrope_section = tuple(mrope_section) self.max_pos = max_pos inv_freq = _build_inv_freq(D) pos = torch.arange(max_pos, dtype=torch.float32) freqs = torch.outer(pos, inv_freq) emb = torch.cat([freqs, freqs], dim=-1) self.register_buffer("cos_cache", emb.cos().to(torch.bfloat16)) self.register_buffer("sin_cache", emb.sin().to(torch.bfloat16)) def forward(self, q, k, pos_t, pos_h, pos_w): B, T, Hq, Hkv, D = self.B, self.T, self.Hq, self.Hkv, self.D q_rot = torch.empty(B, Hq, T, D, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=q.device) k_rot = torch.empty(B, Hkv, T, D, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=k.device) # Process q mrope_fused(q, q_rot, pos_t, pos_h, pos_w, self.cos_cache, self.sin_cache, self.mrope_section, B, T, Hq, D) # Process k mrope_fused(k, k_rot, pos_t, pos_h, pos_w, self.cos_cache, self.sin_cache, self.mrope_section, B, T, Hkv, D) return q_rot, k_rot B = 1 T = 4096 Hq = 32 Hkv = 8 D = 128 MROPE_SECTION = (16, 24, 24) MAX_POS = 32768 def get_inputs(): q = torch.randn(B, T, Hq, D, dtype=torch.bfloat16) * 0.5 k = torch.randn(B, T, Hkv, D, dtype=torch.bfloat16) * 0.5 pos_t = torch.randint(0, MAX_POS, (B, T), dtype=torch.int64) pos_h = torch.randint(0, MAX_POS, (B, T), dtype=torch.int64) pos_w = torch.randint(0, MAX_POS, (B, T), dtype=torch.int64) return [q, k, pos_t, pos_h, pos_w] def get_init_inputs(): return [B, T, Hq, Hkv, D, MROPE_SECTION, MAX_POS] ``` Wait, I realize there's a problem with my Triton kernel. Avoid creating intermediate tensors But this still involves multiple kernel launches and memory transfers. A fused kernel is better. Let me write the Triton solution. Actually, you know what, let me also think about a pure CUDA approach that might be simpler. I could write a kernel that uses thrust or cub for the gather, and then a separate kernel for the rotation and transpose. But this is more complex than a single fused kernel. OK, Triton it is. Let me write the code. Let me also think about potential issues: 1. Triton might not be installed -> fall back to CUDA C++ 2. Triton might not support SM120 -> fall back to CUDA C++ 3. The kernel might have numerical issues -> use fp32 accumulation Let me check Triton availability first, then proceed. Actually, since the project uses torch.compile (which uses Triton as a backend), Triton should be available. Let me just proceed. Let me also think about whether I need to handle the case where D is not a power of 2. The shapes have D=64 and D=128, both powers of 2. So BLOCK_D = D works. For the mrope_section: - (16, 24, 24) for D=128: s0=16, s1=24, s2=24. D/2=64. s0+s1=40, s0+s1+s2=64. - (8, 12, 12) for D=64: s0=8, s1=12, s2=12. D/2=32. s0+s1=20, s0+s1+s2=32. These are all valid. The axis boundaries (s0=16 or 8, s0+s1=40 or 20) divide the D/2 dimension into 3 sections. OK, let me now write the solution. I'll write it to the solution.py file in the problem directory. Wait, actually, I just realized I should check if there's an issue with how the partner read works. In the reference: ```python def _rotate_half(x): half = x.shape[-1] // 2 return torch.cat((-x[..., half:], x[..., :half]), dim=-1) ``` So rotate_half(x)[i] for i < half: -x[i + half] rotate_half(x)[i] for i >= half: x[i - half] Then out[i] = x[i] * cos[i] + rotate_half(x)[i] * sin[i] For i < half: out[i] = x[i] * cos[i] + (-x[i + half]) * sin[i] = x[i] * cos[i] - x[i + half] * sin[i] For i >= half: out[i] = x[i] * cos[i] + x[i - half] * sin[i] And since cos[i] = cos[i - half] (cache mirrors), sin[i] = sin[i - half]: For pair (i, i + half) where i < half: out[i] = x[i] * cos[i] - x[i + half] * sin[i] out[i + half] = x[i + half] * cos[i] + x[i] * sin[i] This is the standard complex rotation, as I analyzed before. My kernel is correct. Let me now write the code! Actually, let me also think about whether I should use a CUDA C++ kernel instead of Triton. CUDA C++ would give me: 1. bf16x2 vector loads/stores for q/k input and output 2. Potentially better control over the cos/sin gather (I could prefetch into shared memory) For a bandwidth-bound kernel, the main bottleneck is the cos/sin gather. If I can optimize the gather pattern, the kernel will be faster. In CUDA C++, I could: 1. Have each thread block process multiple (b, h, t) rows that share the same (b, t) position 2. Load the position indices once per block 3. Load the relevant cos/sin cache rows into shared memory 4. Process all heads for the same (b, t) using the shared cos/sin This would reduce the number of global memory accesses for cos/sin by a factor of H (since H heads share the same position). For the main shape (Hq=32, Hkv=8), this would reduce cos/sin global reads by 32x for q and 8x for k. This is a significant optimization! Let me think about this more carefully. For a given (b, t), there are Hq + Hkv heads. Each head needs the same cos/sin values (since cos/sin depend only on (b, t, d), not on h). So the cos/sin values are shared across Hq + Hkv heads. In my current Triton kernel, each program handles one (b, h, t) row and loads cos/sin independently. So the same cos/sin values are loaded Hq + Hkv times. This is wasteful. A better approach: have each thread block process all heads for a given (b, t). Load cos/sin once into shared memory, then all threads in the block use the shared values. In CUDA C++: - Thread block handles (b, t) and all H heads - blockDim.x = D (each thread handles one d index) - Load cos/sin for (b, t) into shared memory (D * 2 * 2 bytes for cos + sin) - Each thread processes one d index for all H heads (sequentially or in parallel) Wait, if blockDim.x = D and we want to process all H heads, we'd need D * H threads per block. For D=128 and H=32, that's 4096 threads per block. The maximum threads per block on modern GPUs is 1024 (or 2048 on some). So this doesn't work directly. Alternative: use a 2D block (D, H'), where H' is a tile of H. For example, H'=8 (8 heads per thread group). Each thread processes D elements for one head. With D=128 threads in one dimension and H'=8 in the other, we get 1024 threads per block. Actually, let me think about this differently. The key insight is that for a given (b, t), all H heads share the same cos/sin. So I should load cos/sin once and reuse across heads. Approach: - Grid: (B * T) blocks (one per (b, t) position) - Block: (H, D/2) threads (each thread handles one head and one pair) - Shared memory: cos[D] + sin[D] (loaded once per block) For D=128, D/2=64. With H=32, block size = 32 * 64 = 2048 threads. This might be too many per block (max is typically 1024). Alternative: split into sub-blocks. - Grid: (B * T * num_sub_blocks) where num_sub_blocks covers all H heads - Each block handles H_TILE heads and D/2 pairs For H_TILE=8 and D/2=64: block size = 512. This works. But this adds complexity. Let me think about whether the shared memory optimization is worth it. The cos/sin data for one (b, t) is D * 2 * 2 = 512 bytes (D bf16 for cos, D bf16 for sin). Loading this once vs Hq + Hkv times saves (Hq + Hkv - 1) * 512 bytes of global memory traffic. For the main shape: (32 + 8 - 1) * 512 = 39 * 512 = 19,968 bytes saved per (b, t). Total savings: B * T * 19,968 = 1 * 4096 * 19,968 = 81.8 MB. The total data movement without this optimization is ~90 MB (from the formula) + some overhead. So saving 81.8 MB is significant. But wait, the cos/sin data is already cached in L2. Each (b, t) loads at most 3 cache rows (one per axis), each 256 bytes. Total cos/sin data per (b, t) = 3 * 256 * 2 (cos+sin) = 1536 bytes. With L2 caching, the data is read from L2 on subsequent accesses, not from DRAM. So the DRAM savings are less than 81.8 MB. The actual DRAM savings depend on the L2 hit rate. For the main shape: - Total unique cos/sin data: 3 axes * B * T unique positions * D * 2 bytes (cos) + same for sin = 6 * 4096 * 256 = 6.3 MB. This fits easily in L2 (RTX PRO 6000 has a large L2, probably 64-128 MB). - So all cos/sin data is read from DRAM once and then served from L2. With the current approach (no shared memory), each (b, h, t) loads cos/sin from L2. The L2 is fast enough that the overhead is minimal. The DRAM traffic is the same regardless of whether we use shared memory. So the shared memory optimization mainly helps with L2 bandwidth, not DRAM bandwidth. Since the kernel is DRAM-bandwidth-bound, the L2 bandwidth optimization might not help much. However, reducing L2 accesses can still help because: 1. L2 bandwidth is finite and might be a bottleneck 2. Fewer memory instructions means less overhead Let me try the simple approach first (no shared memory, Triton kernel) and see if the performance is good enough. If not, I'll switch to CUDA C++ with shared memory. OK, let me finally write the code. Actually, hold on. Let me reconsider the approach entirely. Maybe I should use a CUDA C++ kernel with the following strategy: 1. One kernel for q, one for k (different H values) 2. Each thread block handles one (b, t) and processes all H heads 3. Shared memory for cos/sin 4. bf16x2 vector loads/stores for q/k Thread organization: - Grid: (B * T) blocks - Block: (D/2, H) threads, where D/2 is the inner dimension (x) and H is the outer dimension (y) - Each thread (tx, ty) handles pair tx for head ty For D=128, D/2=64, H=32: block = (64, 32) = 2048 threads. Too many. For D=128, D/2=64, H=8: block = (64, 8) = 512 threads. OK. But Hq=32 doesn't fit. I'd need to split into multiple sub-blocks or reduce the tile size. Alternative: block = (D/2, H_TILE) where H_TILE covers H in multiple passes. - Grid: (B * T * ceil(H / H_TILE)) blocks - Block: (D/2, H_TILE) threads - Each block handles H_TILE heads for one (b, t) For H_TILE=4, D/2=64: block = (64, 4) = 256 threads. For Hq=32: ceil(32/4) = 8 sub-blocks. Total blocks: B * T * 8 = 32,768. This is workable. But it adds complexity. You know what, let me just use the simple approach: one pair per thread, no shared memory. This is the Triton approach I described earlier. If performance is good enough (within, say, 50% of peak bandwidth), I'll declare victory. If not, I'll switch to the optimized CUDA C++ approach. OK, let me write the Triton solution now. For real. Wait, actually, I want to think about one more thing. The benchmark measures achieved TFLOPS and GB/s, and the peak_fraction is based on bandwidth (since regime=memory). So the score depends on how close we get to 1.8 TB/s. The theoretical minimum bytes per call (from bytes_formula): 4 * B * T * (Hq + Hkv) * D + 12 * B * T * D + 24 * B * T For shape 0: ~90.3 MB If the kernel moves exactly this much data and nothing more, at 1.8 TB/s: Time = 90.3 MB / 1.8 TB/s = 0.050 ms The benchmark formula for GB/s: bytes_moved / time. So: GB/s = 90.3 MB / 0.050 ms = 1806 GB/s = 1.8 TB/s (100% peak) If my kernel moves 2x the theoretical minimum: Time = 180.6 MB / 1.8 TB/s = 0.100 ms GB/s = 90.3 MB / 0.100 ms = 903 GB/s (50% peak) So I need to keep the extra memory traffic low to get a good score. The extra traffic in my kernel comes from: 1. Reading the partner values (x[d + half]): these are part of the same row, already in cache. So the extra DRAM traffic is zero (served from L1/L2). 2. The cos/sin values are gathered from cache, which is also in L2. Extra DRAM traffic is minimal. 3. The position indices are small and cached. So the actual DRAM traffic should be close to the theoretical minimum. The main overhead is the instruction count and memory latency, which affects throughput but not the total data volume. Wait, actually, the DRAM traffic depends on what's in cache. If the entire working set fits in L2, then only the first access to each cache line goes to DRAM. Subsequent accesses are served from L2. The total DRAM traffic is determined by the unique cache lines accessed. For the q input: B * T * Hq * D * 2 bytes = 33.5 MB. This doesn't fit in L2 (well, it might on the RTX PRO 6000 which has a large L2). For the k input: 8.4 MB. For the cos/sin cache: up to 16.8 MB. Total: ~59 MB. The RTX PRO 6000 (Blackwell) has a large L2 cache. The RTX 4090 has 72 MB L2. The RTX PRO 6000 might have even more. So the entire working set might fit in L2! If the working set fits in L2, then the second pass through the data (reading partner values, re-reading cos/sin) is served from L2, not DRAM. The DRAM traffic is just the initial load of the data. In that case, my simple Triton kernel should achieve close to peak bandwidth, because: 1. The initial data load streams through DRAM at peak bandwidth 2. The partner reads and cos/sin re-reads hit L2 3. The output write streams to DRAM at peak bandwidth The total DRAM traffic would be close to the theoretical minimum (q + k + cos/sin cache + output ≈ 90 MB). So the Triton kernel should work well. Let me write it. OK I'm going to stop deliberating and just write the code. Here we go. Let me also consider if I want to try a pure PyTorch optimized version as a baseline. Something like: ```python def forward(self, q, k, pos_t, pos_h, pos_w): B, T, Hq, Hkv, D = self.B, self.T, self.Hq, self.Hkv, self.D s0, s1, s2 = self.mrope_section half = D // 2 # Pre-gather and mix cos/sin cos_t = self.cos_cache[pos_t] # (B, T, D) sin_t = self.sin_cache[pos_t] cos_h = self.cos_cache[pos_h] sin_h = self.sin_cache[pos_h] cos_w = self.cos_cache[pos_w] sin_w = self.sin_cache[pos_w] # Mix axes directly into (B, T, 1, D) for broadcasting # Build index tensor for which axis each d belongs to # This is complex to do efficiently in pure PyTorch... ``` Actually, this would be slower than the reference because it creates the same intermediate tensors. Let me skip this and go straight to Triton. Let me write the solution. Hmm, I realize I should also consider whether the Triton kernel will handle the gather from cos/sin cache efficiently. The gather pattern is: - For d in [0, s0): pos = pos_t[bt] (temporal axis) - For d in [s0, s0+s1): pos = pos_h[bt] (height axis) - For d in [s0+s1, half): pos = pos_w[bt] (width axis) - For d in [half, half+s0): pos = pos_t[bt] (temporal axis, mirrored) - For d in [half+s0, half+s0+s1): pos = pos_h[bt] (height axis, mirrored) - For d in [half+s0+s1, D): pos = pos_w[bt] (width axis, mirrored) So cos_val[d] = cos_cache[pos[d], d] where pos[d] depends on the axis section. In Triton, the load `tl.load(COS_PTR + pos * D + d, ...)` with vector `pos` and vector `d` creates a gather pattern. Since `pos` takes at most 3 unique values (pt, ph, pw), the gather targets at most 3 unique rows of the cache. Triton should handle this reasonably well, but it might not be as efficient as a hand-coded CUDA kernel that loads each row separately. One optimization: load the 3 cache rows separately and then select based on the axis: ```python cos_t_row = tl.load(COS_PTR + pt * D + d, mask=mask) # entire row cos_h_row = tl.load(COS_PTR + ph * D + d, mask=mask) cos_w_row = tl.load(COS_PTR + pw * D + d, mask=mask) cos_val = tl.where(d_in_half < S0, cos_t_row, tl.where(d_in_half < S0 + S1, cos_h_row, cos_w_row)) ``` This loads 3 full rows of D bf16 values each, then selects based on the axis. The total data loaded is 3 * D * 2 = 768 bytes for cos (and same for sin). But we only use D values out of the 3*D loaded. Wait, actually, we use all D values: s0 from t, s1 from h, s2 from w. And these are mirrored for the second half. So we load 3 rows and use D values total. The overhead is loading 2*D extra values (from the rows we don't fully use). But this is simpler and avoids the gather pattern. The 3 loads are contiguous (each row is D consecutive bf16 values), which is efficient. The total data loaded per program: - 3 cos rows: 3 * D * 2 = 768 bytes - 3 sin rows: 3 * D * 2 = 768 bytes - q row: D * 2 = 256 bytes - q partner: D * 2 = 256 bytes - Output: D * 2 = 256 bytes - Position indices: 3 * 8 = 24 bytes Total: ~2328 bytes per program With the gather approach: - cos: D * 2 = 256 bytes (but scattered across 3 rows) - sin: D * 2 = 256 bytes (scattered) - q: D * 2 = 256 bytes (contiguous) - q partner: D * 2 = 256 bytes (contiguous but offset) - Output: D * 2 = 256 bytes (contiguous) - Position indices: 3 * 8 = 24 bytes Total: ~1048 bytes per program The 3-row approach loads more data (2328 vs 1048 bytes), but the loads are contiguous and avoid the gather pattern. For L2-cached data, the contiguous loads might be faster. Actually, I think the gather approach is fine. Triton handles gathers reasonably well, and the data is in L2 cache. Let me use the gather approach for less total data movement. But actually, let me think about the memory access pattern more carefully. With the gather approach: - cos_val[d] is loaded from COS_PTR + pos[d] * D + d - For d in [0, s0): address = COS_PTR + pt * D + d - For d in [s0, s0+s1): address = COS_PTR + ph * D + d - For d in [s0+s1, half): address = COS_PTR + pw * D + d Within each section, the addresses are contiguous: [COS_PTR + pt*D, COS_PTR + pt*D + s0), [COS_PTR + ph*D + s0, COS_PTR + ph*D + s0+s1), etc. So the gather is actually 3 contiguous segments within a warp. Triton should detect this and issue 3 vectorized loads. This is efficient! OK, let me use the original gather approach. It's both less data and efficient access. Let me now write the final code. I promise this is the last analysis before coding. Actually, one more thing. Let me think about whether I should use `tl.load` with `eviction_policy='evict_last'` or other cache hints. For the cos/sin cache, which is read-only and shared across many programs, I want it to stay in cache. Using `eviction_policy='evict_last'` (or in Triton, `cache_modifier`) tells the hardware to keep the data in L2 longer. In Triton, I can pass `cache_modifier` to `tl.load`: ```python cos_val = tl.load(COS_PTR + pos * D + d, mask=mask, cache_modifier='.ca') ``` Wait, I think Triton uses `eviction_policy` parameter. +"""Fused multi-axis RoPE kernel (Triton).
+
+One kernel per tensor (q, k). Each program handles one (b, h, t) row,
+fusing: position-index gather, per-axis cos/sin mix, rotate-half, and
+(B,T,H,D) -> (B,H,T,D) transpose.
+"""
+import torch
+import torch.nn as nn
+import triton
+import triton.language as tl
+
+
+@triton.jit
+def _mrope_kernel(
+    X_PTR, OUT_PTR,
+    POS_T_PTR, POS_H_PTR, POS_W_PTR,
+    COS_PTR, SIN_PTR,
+    n_rows,
+    T: tl.constexpr,
+    H: tl.constexpr,
+    D: tl.constexpr,
+    HALF: tl.constexpr,
+    S0: tl.constexpr,
+    S1: tl.constexpr,
+    BLOCK_D: tl.constexpr,
+):
+    pid = tl.program_id(0)
+    if pid >= n_rows:
+        return
+
+    # decode (b, h, t) from row-major (B, H, T)
+    t_idx = pid % T
+    r = pid // T
+    h_idx = r % H
+    b_idx = r // H
+
+    d = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_D)
+    mask = d < D
+
+    # position indices for this (b, t)
+    bt = b_idx * T + t_idx
+    pt = tl.load(POS_T_PTR + bt)
+    ph = tl.load(POS_H_PTR + bt)
+    pw = tl.load(POS_W_PTR + bt)
+
+    # per-element axis selection -> gather address
+    d_in_half = d % HALF
+    pos = tl.where(d_in_half < S0, pt,
+          tl.where(d_in_half < S0 + S1, ph, pw))
+
+    cos_val = tl.load(COS_PTR + pos * D + d, mask=mask, other=0.0)
+    sin_val = tl.load(SIN_PTR + pos * D + d, mask=mask, other=0.0)
+
+    # read input row from (B, T, H, D)
+    x_base = (b_idx * T + t_idx) * H * D + h_idx * D
+    x0 = tl.load(X_PTR + x_base + d, mask=mask, other=0.0)
+
+    # partner for rotate-half
+    partner_d = tl.where(d < HALF, d + HALF, d - HALF)
+    x1 = tl.load(X_PTR + x_base + partner_d, mask=mask, other=0.0)
+
+    # rotate:  out[d<Half] = x0*c - x1*s
+    #          out[d>=Half] = x1*c + x0*s
+    sign = tl.where(d < HALF, -1.0, 1.0)
+    out = x0 * cos_val + sign * x1 * sin_val
+
+    # write output in (B, H, T, D)
+    out_base = (b_idx * H + h_idx) * T * D + t_idx * D
+    tl.store(OUT_PTR + out_base + d, out, mask=mask)
+
+
+def _launch(x, out, pos_t, pos_h, pos_w, cos_cache, sin_cache,
+            mrope_section, B, T, H, D):
+    s0, s1, _ = mrope_section
+    half = D // 2
+    block_d = triton.next_power_of_2(D)
+    n_rows = B * H * T
+    grid = (n_rows,)
+    _mrope_kernel[grid](
+        x, out,
+        pos_t, pos_h, pos_w,
+        cos_cache, sin_cache,
+        n_rows,
+        T=T, H=H, D=D, HALF=half,
+        S0=s0, S1=s1,
+        BLOCK_D=block_d,
+    )
+
+
+# ---------- public interface (matches reference.py) ----------
+
+OP_TYPE = "rope"
+SUPPORTED_PRECISIONS = ["bf16"]
+HARDWARE_REQUIRED = ["RTX_PRO_6000"]
+
+
+def _build_inv_freq(D: int, base: float = 10000.0) -> torch.Tensor:
+    return 1.0 / (base ** (torch.arange(0, D, 2, dtype=torch.float32) / D))
+
+
+class Model(nn.Module):
+    def __init__(self, B: int, T: int, Hq: int, Hkv: int, D: int,
+                 mrope_section: tuple[int, int, int], max_pos: int):
+        super().__init__()
+        assert sum(mrope_section) == D // 2
+        self.B, self.T = B, T
+        self.Hq, self.Hkv, self.D = Hq, Hkv, D
+        self.mrope_section = tuple(mrope_section)
+        self.max_pos = max_pos
+
+        inv_freq = _build_inv_freq(D)
+        pos = torch.arange(max_pos, dtype=torch.float32)
+        freqs = torch.outer(pos, inv_freq)
+        emb = torch.cat([freqs, freqs], dim=-1)
+        self.register_buffer("cos_cache", emb.cos().to(torch.bfloat16))
+        self.register_buffer("sin_cache", emb.sin().to(torch.bfloat16))
+
+    def forward(self, q, k, pos_t, pos_h, pos_w):
+        B, T, Hq, Hkv, D = self.B, self.T, self.Hq, self.Hkv, self.D
+        ms = self.mrope_section
+        cc, sc = self.cos_cache, self.sin_cache
+
+        q_rot = torch.empty(B, Hq, T, D, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=q.device)
+        k_rot = torch.empty(B, Hkv, T, D, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=k.device)
+
+        _launch(q, q_rot, pos_t, pos_h, pos_w, cc, sc, ms, B, T, Hq, D)
+        _launch(k, k_rot, pos_t, pos_h, pos_w, cc, sc, ms, B, T, Hkv, D)
+
+        return q_rot, k_rot
+
+
+# module-level shape shims (overwritten per-shape by check.py / benchmark.py)
+B = 1
+T = 4096
+Hq = 32
+Hkv = 8
+D = 128
+MROPE_SECTION = (16, 24, 24)
+MAX_POS = 32768
+
+
+def get_inputs():
+    q = torch.randn(B, T, Hq, D, dtype=torch.bfloat16) * 0.5
+    k = torch.randn(B, T, Hkv, D, dtype=torch.bfloat16) * 0.5
+    pos_t = torch.randint(0, MAX_POS, (B, T), dtype=torch.int64)
+    pos_h = torch.randint(0, MAX_POS, (B, T), dtype=torch.int64)
+    pos_w = torch.randint(0, MAX_POS, (B, T), dtype=torch.int64)
+    return [q, k, pos_t, pos_h, pos_w]
+
+
+def get_init_inputs():
+    return [B, T, Hq, Hkv, D, MROPE_SECTION, MAX_POS]

An alternative is to process a batch of rows (e.g., all t values for a given (b, h)). Let me implement this. I'll use a 2D offset grid: ```python # d offsets: [0, 1, ..., D-1] # t offsets within tile: [0, 1, ..., BLOCK_T-1] d = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_D) t_off = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_T) # For each (t_offset, d), compute the address # This creates a 2D grid: (BLOCK_T, BLOCK_D) ``` Wait, in Triton, I can use tl.arange to create 2D grids: ```python d = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_D)[None, :] # (1, BLOCK_D) t_off = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_T)[:, None] # (BLOCK_T, 1) ``` Or I can reshape them: ```python offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_T * BLOCK_D) d = offs % BLOCK_D t_off = offs // BLOCK_D ``` This is a flat 1D representation of the 2D tile. The total elements per program is BLOCK_T * BLOCK_D. For D=128, BLOCK_D=128, BLOCK_T=4: 512 elements per program. For D=64, BLOCK_D=64, BLOCK_T=4: 256 elements per program. This is better than the single-row approach. But there's a complication: the position indices (and hence cos/sin values) vary across t values. So I need to handle the position gather for each t separately. Let me think about how to structure this: ```python @triton.jit def _mrope_kernel( X_PTR, OUT_PTR, POS_T_PTR, POS_H_PTR, POS_W_PTR, COS_PTR, SIN_PTR, n_rows, # B * H * T total_H: tl.constexpr, T: tl.constexpr, H: tl.constexpr, D: tl.constexpr, HALF: tl.constexpr, S0: tl.constexpr, S1: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_T: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_D: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) TILE_SIZE = BLOCK_T # rows per program n_tiles = tl.cdiv(n_rows, TILE_SIZE) if pid >= n_tiles: return row_start = pid * TILE_SIZE # 2D offsets: t_idx within tile, d within row offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_T * BLOCK_D) d = offs % BLOCK_D t_in_tile = offs // BLOCK_D row = row_start + t_in_tile # (BLOCK_T * BLOCK_D,) row indices mask = row < n_rows d_mask = d < D valid = mask & d_mask # Decode (b, h, t) from row t_idx = row % T r = row // T h_idx = r % H b_idx = r // H # Position indices bt = b_idx * T + t_idx pt = tl.load(POS_T_PTR + bt, mask=mask, other=0) ph = tl.load(POS_H_PTR + bt, mask=mask, other=0) pw = tl.load(POS_W_PTR + bt, mask=mask, other=0) # Axis selection d_in_half = d % HALF pos = tl.where(d_in_half < S0, pt, tl.where(d_in_half < S0 + S1, ph, pw)) # Gather cos/sin cos_val = tl.load(COS_PTR + pos * D + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) sin_val = tl.load(SIN_PTR + pos * D + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) # Read input x_base = (b_idx * T + t_idx) * H * D + h_idx * D x0 = tl.load(X_PTR + x_base + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) # Partner partner_d = tl.where(d < HALF, d + HALF, d - HALF) x1 = tl.load(X_PTR + x_base + partner_d, mask=valid, other=0.0) # Rotate sign = tl.where(d < HALF, -1.0, 1.0) out = x0 * cos_val + sign * x1 * sin_val # Write output out_base = (b_idx * H + h_idx) * T * D + t_idx * D tl.store(OUT_PTR + out_base + d, out, mask=valid) ``` Hmm, this is more complex but should give better performance for small shapes. Wait, there's an issue. The `row` indices might span multiple (b, h) groups. For example, if T=2048 and BLOCK_T=4, then row 0 to 3 might all have the same (b=0, h=0) with t=0,1,2,3. Actually, with my row encoding (b * H + h) * T + t, consecutive rows are: - Row 0: b=0, h=0, t=0 - Row 1: b=0, h=0, t=1 - ... - Row T-1: b=0, h=0, t=T-1 - Row T: b=0, h=1, t=0 - ... So consecutive BLOCK_T rows within the same (b, h) group share (b, h). The only issue is at the boundary where we cross from one (b, h) group to the next. If BLOCK_T divides T evenly (which it does for T=2048, 4096, 8192, 16384 with BLOCK_T=4), then each tile is entirely within one (b, h) group. The position indices vary across t values within the tile, but (b, h) is constant. This means the input addresses `(b_idx * T + t_idx) * H * D + h_idx * D + d` are contiguous in memory (varying only t and d), which is good for coalesced access. Let me implement this. Actually, I realize there's another issue. When BLOCK_T > 1 and the tile spans multiple (b, h) groups, the h_idx and b_idx values are different for different rows in the tile. This means the input addresses are not contiguous, which hurts coalescing. But as I noted, if BLOCK_T divides T, each tile is within one (b, h) group. Triton overhead Let me try a different approach: use a single CUDA C++ kernel that processes both q and k, with more work per thread. Actually, let me first try increasing BLOCK_T to see if it helps. I can combine q and k processing in a single kernel Let me try the CUDA C++ approach. Actually, let me first try a simple optimization: process both q and k in a single Triton kernel. This eliminates one kernel launch. But the issue is that q and k have different H values. I'd need to linearize over B * (Hq + Hkv) * T rows and determine whether each row is q or k. This is what my original CUDA C++ design did. Let me implement it in Triton. Actually, there's a problem. In Triton, the constexpr parameters (H, D, etc.) need to be known at compile time. If I process both q and k in one kernel, H changes from Hq to Hkv at some boundary. This means I can't use H as a constexpr. I could pass H as a runtime parameter, but then I lose some optimizations. Alternative: launch two kernels, one for q and one for k. This is what I'm already doing. The kernel launch overhead should be negligible (microseconds). So this shouldn't be the bottleneck. Let me focus on the per-program work. For shape 2 with D=64: - Each program processes 1 row of D=64 elements - That's 128 bytes of data per program - With 73,728 programs, total data is ~9.4 MB At 1800 GB/s, reading 9.4 MB takes 0.005 ms. But the actual time is 0.051 ms, which is 10x slower. The overhead might come from: 1. Triton JIT overhead (unlikely after warmup) 2. Memory access pattern (cos/sin gather is scattered) 3. Instruction overhead (too many instructions per element) 4. Low occupancy (too few threads per block) Let me check: with Triton, BLOCK_D=64 means 64/2 = 32 threads per program (Triton processes 2 bf16 values per thread in a warp). 32 threads = 1 warp. Each SM can run multiple warps concurrently, but a single warp might not be enough to hide memory latency. The RTX PRO 6000 has ~170 SMs (estimated). With 73,728 programs, each SM runs ~433 programs. Each program is 1 warp (32 threads). The max threads per SM is 2048, so 64 warps per SM. With 433 programs/SM, we're well within limits. But the issue might be that each program is too small (1 warp) to keep the memory pipeline busy. The memory pipeline can have many outstanding requests, but a single warp can only have a limited number of outstanding loads. Solution: increase the work per program (more warps per program). Using BLOCK_T > 1 would help. Let me try BLOCK_T=8 (process 8 rows per program). With D=64, that's 8 * 64 = 512 elements = 1024 bytes per program. Actually, in Triton, I can't easily do 2D tiles. Let me think about how to implement this. One approach: flatten the tile into 1D. Each program handles BLOCK_T * D elements. ```python @triton.jit def _mrope_kernel( X_PTR, OUT_PTR, POS_T_PTR, POS_H_PTR, POS_W_PTR, COS_PTR, SIN_PTR, B: tl.constexpr, T: tl.constexpr, H: tl.constexpr, D: tl.constexpr, HALF: tl.constexpr, S0: tl.constexpr, S1: tl.constexpr, BLOCK: tl.constexpr, # BLOCK_T * BLOCK_D BLOCK_T: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_D: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) # This program handles BLOCK_T rows starting from row pid * BLOCK_T # But rows are in (B, H, T) order base_row = pid * BLOCK_T offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK) # flat index within tile d = offs % BLOCK_D t_in_tile = offs // BLOCK_D # which row within the tile (0..BLOCK_T-1) row = base_row + t_in_tile n_rows = B * H * T valid = (row < n_rows) & (d < D) # Decode (b, h, t_idx) from row t_idx = row % T r = row // T h_idx = r % H b_idx = r // H # Position bt = b_idx * T + t_idx pt = tl.load(POS_T_PTR + bt, mask=row < n_rows, other=0) ph = tl.load(POS_H_PTR + bt, mask=row < n_rows, other=0) pw = tl.load(POS_W_PTR + bt, mask=row < n_rows, other=0) # Axis d_in_half = d % HALF pos = tl.where(d_in_half < S0, pt, tl.where(d_in_half < S0 + S1, ph, pw)) cos_val = tl.load(COS_PTR + pos * D + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) sin_val = tl.load(SIN_PTR + pos * D + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) # Input (B, T, H, D) x_base = (b_idx * T + t_idx) * H * D + h_idx * D x0 = tl.load(X_PTR + x_base + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) partner_d = tl.where(d < HALF, d + HALF, d - HALF) x1 = tl.load(X_PTR + x_base + partner_d, mask=valid, other=0.0) sign = tl.where(d < HALF, -1.0, 1.0) out = x0 * cos_val + sign * x1 * sin_val # Output (B, H, T, D) out_base = (b_idx * H + h_idx) * T * D + t_idx * D tl.store(OUT_PTR + out_base + d, out, mask=valid) ``` Wait, there's a problem with the position load. When I use `mask=row < n_rows`, the `row` is a vector (different for each element in the tile). This means some elements might be masked out (invalid rows). But I need to load the position for each valid row, and the position is the same for all d values within a row. In the tiled approach, different t_in_tile values have different positions. So I need to load one position per t_in_tile value. The mask for the position load should be `t_in_tile < actual_tile_size` (i.e., the row is valid). Let me restructure: ```python offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK) # BLOCK_T * BLOCK_D d = offs % BLOCK_D t_in_tile = offs // BLOCK_D base_row = pid * BLOCK_T row = base_row + t_in_tile n_rows = B * H * T row_valid = row < n_rows d_valid = d < D valid = row_valid & d_valid # Decode t_idx = row % T r = row // T h_idx = r % H b_idx = r // H # Position bt = b_idx * T + t_idx pt = tl.load(POS_T_PTR + bt, mask=row_valid, other=0) ph = tl.load(POS_H_PTR + bt, mask=row_valid, other=0) pw = tl.load(POS_W_PTR + bt, mask=row_valid, other=0) ``` This should work. The position loads use `row_valid` as the mask, which ensures we only load for valid rows. But there's still the issue that when BLOCK_T rows span multiple (b, h) groups, the input addresses are not contiguous. This only happens when the tile crosses a (b, h) boundary, which occurs when T doesn't divide evenly by BLOCK_T or when the tile starts near the end of a (b, h) group. For T=2048 and BLOCK_T=8, each (b, h) group has 2048 rows. So tiles of 8 rows are entirely within one group (8 divides 2048). The input addresses are contiguous for all rows in the tile. For T=4096 and BLOCK_T=8, same thing. All tiles are within one group. Good. Let me proceed with BLOCK_T=8. Actually, I realize there's another subtlety. When BLOCK_T=8 and BLOCK_D=64, BLOCK=512. In Triton, the block size (number of threads) is typically limited to 1024 or 2048. BLOCK=512 should be fine. But with BLOCK=512 bf16 elements, that's 1024 bytes per program. Triton might split this into multiple warps (512/32 = 16 warps per program). This is better than 1 warp per program. Actually, wait. Triton doesn't necessarily use 1 thread per element. With 32 threads per warp, that's 8 warps per program. This is much better than 1 warp. For D=128, BLOCK_T=4: BLOCK=512 elements, 256 threads, 8 warps. Good. Actually, let me also try a different optimization: loading cos/sin for all 3 axes separately, then selecting per-element. This avoids the gather pattern: ```python # Load full rows for each axis cos_t = tl.load(COS_PTR + pt * D + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) sin_t = tl.load(SIN_PTR + pt * D + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) cos_h = tl.load(COS_PTR + ph * D + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) sin_h = tl.load(SIN_PTR + ph * D + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) cos_w = tl.load(COS_PTR + pw * D + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) sin_w = tl.load(SIN_PTR + pw * D + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) # Select per-element cos_val = tl.where(d_in_half < S0, cos_t, tl.where(d_in_half < S0 + S1, cos_h, cos_w)) sin_val = tl.where(d_in_half < S0, sin_t, tl.where(d_in_half < S0 + S1, sin_h, sin_w)) ``` This loads 6 rows (3 cos + 3 sin) instead of gathering from the mix. The total data loaded is 6 * D * 2 bytes. For D=128: 1536 bytes. For D=64: 768 bytes. With the gather approach, I load 2 * D * 2 bytes (cos + sin, but gathered from 3 rows). But the gather might be slower due to non-contiguous access. The 6-row approach loads more data but with contiguous access patterns. For L2-cached data, the extra bandwidth is cheap. Let me try both and see which is faster. Actually, I think the 6-row approach might be faster because: 1. Each load is contiguous (D consecutive bf16 values) 2. No gather pattern -> better coalescing 3. The extra data (6*D vs 2*D) is served from L2 cache, so minimal DRAM impact Let me try the 6-row approach. OK, let me write a new version of the kernel with both optimizations: 1. Tiled processing (BLOCK_T rows per program) 2. 6-row cos/sin loading (no gather) Here's the plan: ```python @triton.jit def _mrope_kernel( X_PTR, OUT_PTR, POS_T_PTR, POS_H_PTR, POS_W_PTR, COS_PTR, SIN_PTR, B: tl.constexpr, T: tl.constexpr, H: tl.constexpr, D: tl.constexpr, HALF: tl.constexpr, S0: tl.constexpr, S1: tl.constexpr, BLOCK: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_T: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_D: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) base_row = pid * BLOCK_T offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK) d = offs % BLOCK_D t_in_tile = offs // BLOCK_D row = base_row + t_in_tile n_rows = B * H * T row_valid = row < n_rows valid = row_valid & (d < D) # Decode (b, h, t) t_idx = row % T r = row // T h_idx = r % H b_idx = r // H # Position bt = b_idx * T + t_idx pt = tl.load(POS_T_PTR + bt, mask=row_valid, other=0) ph = tl.load(POS_H_PTR + bt, mask=row_valid, other=0) pw = tl.load(POS_W_PTR + bt, mask=row_valid, other=0) # Load cos/sin for all 3 axes (each is contiguous) # pt, ph, pw are per-row (vary with t_in_tile), so we need to broadcast d # pt is shape (BLOCK,), d is shape (BLOCK,), so pt * D + d is shape (BLOCK,) cos_t = tl.load(COS_PTR + pt * D + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) sin_t = tl.load(SIN_PTR + pt * D + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) cos_h = tl.load(COS_PTR + ph * D + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) sin_h = tl.load(SIN_PTR + ph * D + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) cos_w = tl.load(COS_PTR + pw * D + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) sin_w = tl.load(SIN_PTR + pw * D + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) # Select per-element d_in_half = d % HALF cos_val = tl.where(d_in_half < S0, cos_t, tl.where(d_in_half < S0 + S1, cos_h, cos_w)) sin_val = tl.where(d_in_half < S0, sin_t, tl.where(d_in_half < S0 + S1, sin_h, sin_w)) # Input x_base = (b_idx * T + t_idx) * H * D + h_idx * D x0 = tl.load(X_PTR + x_base + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) partner_d = tl.where(d < HALF, d + HALF, d - HALF) x1 = tl.load(X_PTR + x_base + partner_d, mask=valid, other=0.0) sign = tl.where(d < HALF, -1.0, 1.0) out = x0 * cos_val + sign * x1 * sin_val # Output out_base = (b_idx * H + h_idx) * T * D + t_idx * D tl.store(OUT_PTR + out_base + d, out, mask=valid) ``` Hmm wait, there's an issue with the `pt * D + d` addressing. `pt` varies with t_in_tile (different rows have different positions). `d` varies within the row. So `pt * D + d` is a per-element offset, and different elements might access different cache rows. But within a single row (same t_in_tile), the access pattern is: pt * D + 0, pt * D + 1, ..., pt * D + D-1. This is a contiguous range of D elements = 1 cache row. Good. For the 6 loads (cos_t, sin_t, cos_h, sin_h, cos_w, sin_w), each accesses D consecutive elements from a cache row. The total data per program is 6 * D * 2 = 768 bytes for D=64, or 1536 bytes for D=128. This is larger than the 2 * D * 2 = 256/512 bytes with the gather approach. But the access pattern is more regular. Wait, I think there's another issue. When BLOCK_T > 1, different rows in the tile might have different position indices. So `pt` is a vector of BLOCK_T different values. The load `COS_PTR + pt * D + d` accesses BLOCK_T different cache rows, each at D consecutive elements. For example, with BLOCK_T=8: - pt = [pt0, pt0, ..., pt0, pt1, pt1, ..., pt1, ..., pt7, pt7, ..., pt7] (BLOCK_D copies of each pt value) - d = [0, 1, ..., D-1, 0, 1, ..., D-1, ..., 0, 1, ..., D-1] (repeating pattern for each row) So the access pattern is: - Elements 0..D-1: COS_PTR + pt0 * D + [0..D-1] (row pt0) - Elements D..2D-1: COS_PTR + pt1 * D + [0..D-1] (row pt1) - ... Each segment of D elements accesses a different cache row. The hardware handles this as BLOCK_T separate contiguous loads. In Triton, this is represented as a single `tl.load` with a vector address. The hardware will coalesce consecutive elements into as few memory transactions as possible. Since each segment of D elements is contiguous, the load is efficient. The extra data (6*D*2 vs 2*D*2) is 4*D*2 = 512 bytes for D=64, 1024 bytes for D=128. This is small enough to fit in L1. I think the 6-row approach is better for regularity. Let me use it. Actually, for memory coalescing, what matters is that threads within a warp access addresses in the same cache line. With the tiled approach: - Thread 0: element at (row 0, d=0) - Thread 1: element at (row 0, d=1) - ... - Thread D-1: element at (row 0, d=D-1) - Thread D: element at (row 1, d=0) - ... The actual thread mapping depends on Triton's internal lowering. But typically, Triton tries to map consecutive elements to consecutive threads for coalescing. With the flat offset `offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK)` where BLOCK = BLOCK_T * BLOCK_D: - offs = [0, 1, ..., BLOCK-1] - d = offs % BLOCK_D = [0, 1, ..., BLOCK_D-1, 0, 1, ..., BLOCK_D-1, ...] - t_in_tile = offs // BLOCK_D = [0, 0, ..., 0, 1, 1, ..., 1, ...] So the first BLOCK_D elements are row 0, next BLOCK_D are row 1, etc. If BLOCK_D = D = 64 or 128, consecutive threads access consecutive d values within the same row.
