Actually, let me check: with constexpr H and T, the compiler can: 1. Optimize the modulo operations (`row % T`, `r % H`) into multiply-shift if T and H are powers of 2 2. Unroll loops based on T and H For T=4096 (= 2^12) and H=32 (= 2^5), the modulo is a simple bitmask. This might give a small speedup. But for non-power-of-2 values like Hq=28, the modulo is a full division, which is slow. So making H and T constexpr doesn't help much. Let me make only D, HALF, S0, S1, BLOCK_D, BLOCK_T, and BLOCK constexpr. H and T can be runtime. This reduces compilations to: - D=128, HALF=64, S0=16, S1=24, BLOCK_D=128, BLOCK_T=4, BLOCK=512 -> 1 compilation - D=64, HALF=32, S0=8, S1=12, BLOCK_D=64, BLOCK_T=8, BLOCK=512 -> 1 compilation Only 2 compilations. This is much better. But wait, for the k kernel with Hkv=2 and D=64, I still use the same kernel compiled with D=64. The H value is passed as a runtime parameter. This should work. OK, let me finalize the design: - D, HALF, S0, S1, BLOCK_D, BLOCK_T, BLOCK: constexpr - B, T, H, n_rows: runtime Let me write the code. Hmm, actually, I'm not sure if Triton handles the `%` operation efficiently for non-constexpr values. If T is runtime, `row % T` is a full modulo operation, which is expensive. If T is constexpr and a power of 2, it's a bitmask. For T=4096 (= 2^12), the bitmask is very fast. For T=8192 (= 2^13), also fast. For T=2048 (= 2^11) and T=16384 (= 2^14), all powers of 2. So making T constexpr is beneficial. But it increases the number of compilations. Let me check: T values are 4096, 8192, 2048, 16384. Each is a power of 2. If I make T constexpr, I get 4 * 2 (D values) = 8 compilations (for q and k separately). But actually, q and k use different H values, so if H is also constexpr: 4 * 2 (D) * 6 (H values) = 48 compilations. That's a lot. If I make only D-related values constexpr and T/H runtime: 2 compilations. The modulo operations are slower, but since the kernel is bandwidth-bound, it doesn't matter. OK, let me use: D, HALF, S0, S1, BLOCK_D, BLOCK_T, BLOCK as constexpr. B, T, H, n_rows as runtime. Let me write the code now. For real this time. One final thought: instead of using BLOCK_T, I can just increase BLOCK_D beyond D. For example, BLOCK_D=512 for D=128 processes 4 rows per program. But this changes the memory access pattern. Actually, BLOCK_D must equal D for the d-in-half logic to work. If BLOCK_D > D, the extra elements have d >= D and are masked out. But the tiling logic (t_in_tile = offs // BLOCK_D) doesn't work correctly if BLOCK_D != D. So I need BLOCK_D = D (or next_power_of_2(D), which is D since D is already a power of 2). OK, let me just write the code with BLOCK_T as a separate constexpr. Let me also think about whether BLOCK_T should be a constexpr or runtime. Since it determines the program's internal structure (BLOCK = BLOCK_T * BLOCK_D), it should be constexpr. But I can pass different values at launch time. OK here's my final approach: ```python @triton.jit def _mrope_kernel( X_PTR, OUT_PTR, POS_T_PTR, POS_H_PTR, POS_W_PTR, COS_PTR, SIN_PTR, n_rows, T_val, H_val, D: tl.constexpr, HALF: tl.constexpr, S0: tl.constexpr, S1: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_D: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_T: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) base_row = pid * BLOCK_T BLOCK = BLOCK_T * BLOCK_D offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK) d = offs % BLOCK_D t_in_tile = offs // BLOCK_D row = base_row + t_in_tile row_valid = row < n_rows d_valid = d < D valid = row_valid & d_valid # Decode (b, h, t) from row in (B, H, T) order t_idx = row % T_val r = row // T_val h_idx = r % H_val b_idx = r // H_val # Position bt = b_idx * T_val + t_idx pt = tl.load(POS_T_PTR + bt, mask=row_valid, other=0) ph = tl.load(POS_H_PTR + bt, mask=row_valid, other=0) pw = tl.load(POS_W_PTR + bt, mask=row_valid, other=0) # Load cos/sin for all 3 axes cos_t = tl.load(COS_PTR + pt * D + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) sin_t = tl.load(SIN_PTR + pt * D + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) cos_h = tl.load(COS_PTR + ph * D + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) sin_h = tl.load(SIN_PTR + ph * D + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) cos_w = tl.load(COS_PTR + pw * D + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) sin_w = tl.load(SIN_PTR + pw * D + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) # Select per-element based on mrope_section d_in_half = d % HALF cos_val = tl.where(d_in_half < S0, cos_t, tl.where(d_in_half < S0 + S1, cos_h, cos_w)) sin_val = tl.where(d_in_half < S0, sin_t, tl.where(d_in_half < S0 + S1, sin_h, sin_w)) # Input (B, T, H, D) layout x_base = (b_idx * T_val + t_idx) * H_val * D + h_idx * D x0 = tl.load(X_PTR + x_base + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) # Partner partner_d = tl.where(d < HALF, d + HALF, d - HALF) x1 = tl.load(X_PTR + x_base + partner_d, mask=valid, other=0.0) # Rotate sign = tl.where(d < HALF, -1.0, 1.0) out = x0 * cos_val + sign * x1 * sin_val # Output (B, H, T, D) layout out_base = (b_idx * H_val + h_idx) * T_val * D + t_idx * D tl.store(OUT_PTR + out_base + d, out, mask=valid) ``` Wait, there's a problem with the `valid` mask for the cos/sin loads. The mask `valid = row_valid & d_valid` ensures we don't load out-of-bounds. But for the 6-row cos/sin loads, we're loading D elements per row. If BLOCK_D = D (which it is for our shapes), then `d_valid` is always true (d < D for all d in [0, BLOCK_D)). So `valid = row_valid`. Wait, BLOCK_D = next_power_of_2(D) = D (since D is a power of 2). So d ranges from 0 to D-1, and d_valid is always true. Actually, for D=64, BLOCK_D=64. d = offs % 64 = [0, 1, ..., 63, 0, 1, ..., 63, ...]. d_valid = d < 64, which is always true. Good. For D=128, BLOCK_D=128. Same thing. d_valid is always true. So `valid = row_valid`. This simplifies the code. OK, one more issue. The `cos_t = tl.load(COS_PTR + pt * D + d, ...)` line: `pt` is a vector with potentially different values for different t_in_tile. And `d` is also a vector. So `pt * D + d` creates a gather pattern. For the first BLOCK_D elements (t_in_tile=0): - pt is the same value (pt0) for all elements - d ranges from 0 to D-1 - Address: COS_PTR + pt0 * D + [0, 1, ..., D-1] -> contiguous For the second BLOCK_D elements (t_in_tile=1): - pt is pt1 - d ranges from 0 to D-1 - Address: COS_PTR + pt1 * D + [0, 1, ..., D-1] -> contiguous (but different from pt0's range) So the load is BLOCK_T contiguous segments, each D elements long. Triton should handle this as BLOCK_T vectorized loads. This is efficient. OK, I'm satisfied with the design. Let me write the final code. Hmm, I realize I need to handle one more thing. The kernel should work for both q and k. Since q has H=Hq and k has H=Hkv, I need to pass H as a parameter. With the tiled approach, the number of rows is B * H * T, and the number of programs is ceil(n_rows / BLOCK_T). But when BLOCK_T doesn't divide n_rows, the last program has fewer rows. The mask `row_valid = row < n_rows` handles this. For the q kernel: n_rows = B * Hq * T. For the k kernel: n_rows = B * Hkv * T. With BLOCK_T=4: - Shape 0 q: n_rows = 1 * 32 * 4096 = 131072, programs = 32768 - Shape 0 k: n_rows = 1 * 8 * 4096 = 32768, programs = 8192 With BLOCK_T=8 for D=64: - Shape 2 q: n_rows = 2 * 16 * 2048 = 65536, programs = 8192 - Shape 2 k: n_rows = 2 * 2 * 2048 = 8192, programs = 1024 These are all fine. OK, let me write the final solution. I'll use the tiled approach with BLOCK_T=4 for D=128 and BLOCK_T=8 for D=64. Wait, actually, I should choose BLOCK_T dynamically based on the problem size. For large problems (many rows), BLOCK_T=4 is fine. For small problems, BLOCK_T=8 or 16 might be better. But since BLOCK_T is a constexpr, I can't change it at runtime. I'll just use BLOCK_T=4 for all cases. For D=64, BLOCK=4*64=256, which gives 128 threads, 4 warps. That's reasonable. Actually, for D=64, BLOCK=256, Triton uses 128 threads. That's 4 warps per program. For shape 2 with 8192 programs (q), total warps = 32768. With 170 SMs and 64 warps/SM, max concurrent warps = 10880. So we can keep all SMs busy. Let me use BLOCK_T=4 for all cases. Simpler code. Wait, but for D=64, BLOCK=256 is only 512 bytes of data per program. That might not be enough to amortize overhead. Let me use BLOCK_T=8 for D=64. Since D is a constexpr, Triton will compile separate versions for D=64 and D=128. Each version can have a different BLOCK_T. OK, final decision: - D=128: BLOCK_T=4, BLOCK=512, BLOCK_D=128 - D=64: BLOCK_T=8, BLOCK=512, BLOCK_D=64 Both have BLOCK=512, which gives 256 threads (8 warps). Consistent. But BLOCK_T is also a constexpr, so the kernel has D, HALF, S0, S1, BLOCK_D, BLOCK_T, BLOCK as constexprs. With 2 sets of constexpr values (D=128 and D=64), we get 2 compilations. The same compiled kernel is reused for all shapes with the same D. Let me write the code now. Oh wait, I also want to handle the case where the BLOCK_T rows span different (b, h) groups. This happens when n_rows is not a multiple of T (i.e., when the tile starts near the end of one group and extends into the next group). With BLOCK_T=4 and T=4096, the groups are exactly T=4096 rows. Tiles of 4 rows are always within one group. So this is fine for all our shapes. But if T were not divisible by BLOCK_T, the last tile of each group would span into the next group. The mask handles this correctly (invalid rows are masked out), but it wastes some threads. For our shapes, T is always divisible by 4 (and by 8). So this isn't an issue. OK, I'm writing the code now. For absolutely real this time. Actually, let me reconsider one more time. Maybe I should try a CUDA C++ kernel instead of Triton. CUDA C++ would give me: 1. bf16x2 loads/stores for q/k input and output 2. Shared memory for cos/sin values shared across threads in a block 3. More control over thread mapping For the main bottleneck (shape 2 with 24% peak), CUDA C++ might give better performance. But writing and debugging CUDA C++ takes more time. Let me try the Triton optimization first, and if it's not enough, switch to CUDA C++. OK, writing the code now.

Let me write the kernel: ```cpp __global__ void __launch_bounds__(512) mrope_kernel( __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ q_rot, // (B, Hq, T, D) __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ k_rot, // (B, Hkv, T, D) const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ q, // (B, T, Hq, D) const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ k, // (B, T, Hkv, D) const int64_t* __restrict__ pos_t, // (B, T) const int64_t* __restrict__ pos_h, // (B, T) const int64_t* __restrict__ pos_w, // (B, T) const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ cos_cache, // (max_pos, D) const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ sin_cache, // (max_pos, D) const int B, const int T, const int Hq, const int Hkv, const int D, const int s0, const int s1, const int s2, const int H_TILE, // heads per block const int is_q // 1 for q, 0 for k ) { const int H = is_q ? Hq : Hkv; const int half_d = D / 2; // Shared memory for cos/sin (6 rows of D bf16 values) extern __shared__ __nv_bfloat16 smem[]; __nv_bfloat16* s_cos_t = smem; __nv_bfloat16* s_sin_t = s_cos_t + D; __nv_bfloat16* s_cos_h = s_sin_t + D; __nv_bfloat16* s_sin_h = s_cos_h + D; __nv_bfloat16* s_cos_w = s_sin_h + D; __nv_bfloat16* s_sin_w = s_cos_w + D; // Total: 6 * D * sizeof(__nv_bfloat16) = 6 * D * 2 bytes // Block handles one (b, t) and H_TILE heads const int n_groups = B * T; const int n_head_tiles = (H + H_TILE - 1) / H_TILE; const int total_blocks = n_groups * n_head_tiles; const int block_id = blockIdx.x; if (block_id >= total_blocks) return; const int group_id = block_id / n_head_tiles; const int head_tile = block_id % n_head_tiles; const int b = group_id / T; const int t = group_id % T; const int h_start = head_tile * H_TILE; // Thread index const int tx = threadIdx.x; // pair index (0..half_d-1) const int ty = threadIdx.y; // head index within tile (0..H_TILE-1) const int tid = ty * blockDim.x + tx; // Load cos/sin into shared memory cooperatively const int64_t pt = pos_t[b * T + t]; const int64_t ph = pos_h[b * T + t]; const int64_t pw = pos_w[b * T + t]; const __nv_bfloat16* cos_t_row = cos_cache + pt * D; const __nv_bfloat16* sin_t_row = sin_cache + pt * D; const __nv_bfloat16* cos_h_row = cos_cache + ph * D; const __nv_bfloat16* sin_h_row = sin_cache + ph * D; const __nv_bfloat16* cos_w_row = cos_cache + pw * D; const __nv_bfloat16* sin_w_row = sin_cache + pw * D; // Cooperative load: 6 * D elements, blockDim.x * blockDim.y threads const int total_threads = blockDim.x * blockDim.y; for (int i = tid; i < 6 * D; i += total_threads) { const int row = i / D; const int d_idx = i % D; switch (row) { case 0: s_cos_t[d_idx] = cos_t_row[d_idx]; break; case 1: s_sin_t[d_idx] = sin_t_row[d_idx]; break; case 2: s_cos_h[d_idx] = cos_h_row[d_idx]; break; case 3: s_sin_h[d_idx] = sin_h_row[d_idx]; break; case 4: s_cos_w[d_idx] = cos_w_row[d_idx]; break; case 5: s_sin_w[d_idx] = sin_w_row[d_idx]; break; } } __syncthreads(); // Each thread processes one pair for one head if (tx >= half_d) return; const int h = h_start + ty; if (h >= H) return; // Determine axis for this d_pair const __nv_bfloat16* c_ptr; const __nv_bfloat16* s_ptr; if (tx < s0) { c_ptr = s_cos_t; s_ptr = s_sin_t; } else if (tx < s0 + s1) { c_ptr = s_cos_h; s_ptr = s_sin_h; } else { c_ptr = s_cos_w; s_ptr = s_sin_w; } const float c = __bfloat162float(c_ptr[tx]); const float s = __bfloat162float(s_ptr[tx]); // Also need the mirrored value for d_pair + half // Since the cache is mirrored, cos[d_pair] == cos[d_pair + half] // So we only need to load from the first half of the shared memory // Read input from (B, T, H, D) layout const __nv_bfloat16* x_base = is_q ? q : k; const int x_off = (b * T + t) * H * D + h * D; const float x0 = __bfloat162float(x_base[x_off + tx]); const float x1 = __bfloat162float(x_base[x_off + tx + half_d]); // Rotate const float out0 = x0 * c - x1 * s; const float out1 = x1 * c + x0 * s; // Write output in (B, H, T, D) layout __nv_bfloat16* out_base = is_q ? q_rot : k_rot; const int out_off = ((b * H + h) * T + t) * D; out_base[out_off + tx] = __float2bfloat16(out0); out_base[out_off + tx + half_d] = __float2bfloat16(out1); } ``` Wait, this kernel has issues: 1. The `is_q` parameter makes it a runtime branch, which is fine for the host but adds a minor overhead in the kernel. 2. The shared memory loading uses a switch statement, which might not be optimal. 3. The cooperative loading loop might have bank conflicts. With BLOCK_T=16 for D=64, each program handles 16 consecutive rows. For the input (B, T, H, D) layout, 16 consecutive rows in (B, H, T) output order correspond to 16 consecutive t values for the same (b, h). Large grid to ensure full GPU utilization Thread mapping: - Linear index over all pairs: B * (Hq + Hkv) * T * D/2 - Each thread handles 2 consecutive pairs - Total threads: B * (Hq + Hkv) * T * D/2 / 2 = B * (Hq + Hkv) * T * D/4 For shape 2: 2 * 18 * 2048 * 64 / 4 = 1,179,648 threads With 256 threads per block: 4608 blocks For shape 0: 1 * 40 * 4096 * 128 / 4 = 5,242,880 threads With 256 threads per block: 20480 blocks These are good numbers. With bf16x2 loads, each thread: 1. Loads x[d] and x[d+1] as bf16x2 (4 bytes, one load) 2. Loads x[d+half] and x[d+half+1] as bf16x2 (4 bytes, one load) 3. Loads cos[pos, d] and cos[pos, d+1] as bf16x2 (if they're from the same axis) 4. Loads sin[pos, d] and sin[pos, d+1] as bf16x2 (same condition) 5. Computes rotation for both pairs 6. Stores out[d] and out[d+1] as bf16x2 (4 bytes, one store) 7. Stores out[d+half] and out[d+half+1] as bf16x2 (4 bytes, one store) The issue is step 3/4: d and d+1 might be in different axis sections (e.g., d=15 and d=16 with s0=16). In that case, they use different position indices, and we can't use bf16x2 for cos/sin. But for q/k input and output, we can always use bf16x2 because the data is contiguous regardless of the axis. So the vectorized approach is: - bf16x2 for input x (2 loads per thread) - scalar for cos/sin (2 loads per pair * 2 pairs = 4 loads per thread) - bf16x2 for output (2 stores per thread) Total memory operations per thread: 2 vector loads + 4 scalar loads + 2 vector stores = 8 operations for 4 bf16 elements. With scalar approach (1 pair per thread): - 2 scalar loads for x - 2 scalar loads for cos/sin - 2 scalar stores for output = 6 operations for 2 bf16 elements. So the vectorized approach does 8/4 = 2 ops/elem, while scalar does 6/2 = 3 ops/elem. The vectorized approach is 50% more efficient. But the cos/sin loads can't be vectorized when d and d+1 cross axis boundaries. This happens at d = s0-1 (e.g., d=15 and d=16 with s0=16) and d = s0+s1-1 (e.g., d=39 and d=40 with s0+s1=40). For D=128, half=64, these are at most 2 boundaries out of 32 pairs per half. So 30/32 = 94% of pairs can use vectorized cos/sin loads. For simplicity, let me just use scalar cos/sin loads and vectorized x loads/stores. This gives: - 2 vector loads for x (4 bf16 values) - 4 scalar loads for cos/sin (4 bf16 values, but individual loads) - 2 vector stores for output (4 bf16 values) Total: 8 load/store operations for 4 output bf16 elements. Actually, let me reconsider. With 2 pairs per thread: - Each pair needs 1 cos and 1 sin value - 2 pairs -> 4 scalar loads for cos/sin - But if d and d+1 are in the same axis section, I can use a bf16x2 load for cos/sin too Let me implement the general case (scalar cos/sin, vectorized x) and see how it performs. Here's the CUDA C++ kernel: ```cpp __global__ void __launch_bounds__(256) mrope_kernel( __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ q_rot, __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ k_rot, const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ q, const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ k, const int64_t* __restrict__ pos_t, const int64_t* __restrict__ pos_h, const int64_t* __restrict__ pos_w, const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ cos_cache, const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ sin_cache, const int B, const int T, const int Hq, const int Hkv, const int D, const int s0, const int s1, const int s2 ) { const int half_d = D / 2; const int total_q = B * Hq * T * half_d; const int total_k = B * Hkv * T * half_d; const int total = total_q + total_k; // Each thread handles 2 pairs const int tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; const int pair_idx = tid * 2; // starting pair index if (pair_idx >= total) return; // Process 2 pairs for (int p = 0; p < 2; p++) { const int idx = pair_idx + p; if (idx >= total) return; const bool is_q = idx < total_q; const int H = is_q ? Hq : Hkv; const int li = is_q ? idx : idx - total_q; const int dp = li % half_d; int r = li / half_d; const int t = r % T; r /= T; const int h = r % H; const int b = r / H; const int bt = b * T + t; int64_t pos; if (dp < s0) pos = pos_t[bt]; else if (dp < s0 + s1) pos = pos_h[bt]; else pos = pos_w[bt]; const float c = __bfloat162float(cos_cache[pos * D + dp]); const float s = __bfloat162float(sin_cache[pos * D + dp]); const __nv_bfloat16* x_base = is_q ? q : k; const int x_off = (b * T + t) * H * D + h * D; const float x0 = __bfloat162float(x_base[x_off + dp]); const float x1 = __bfloat162float(x_base[x_off + dp + half_d]); const float out0 = x0 * c - x1 * s; const float out1 = x1 * c + x0 * s; __nv_bfloat16* out_base = is_q ? q_rot : k_rot; const int out_off = ((b * H + h) * T + t) * D; out_base[out_off + dp] = __float2bfloat16(out0); out_base[out_off + dp + half_d] = __float2bfloat16(out1); } } ``` This is essentially the same as my original CUDA kernel but with 2 pairs per thread. The benefit is fewer threads and more work per thread. But wait, there's a problem. The 2 pairs might belong to different tensors (q vs k) if pair_idx is near total_q. In that case, the loop body would process one q pair and one k pair. The branch `is_q` would diverge within the loop, causing warp divergence. To avoid this, I could process only q or only k per thread. But then I'd need separate kernel launches for q and k. Actually, the warp divergence is only an issue for the few threads near the q/k boundary. Most threads process either all q or all k pairs. The performance impact is negligible. But there's another issue: the 2 pairs might have different d_pair indices (since they're consecutive in the linearized index). If the pairs have different axes, the cos/sin loads are from different cache rows. This is fine for scalar loads but means I can't vectorize. Let me simplify: process 1 pair per thread, but use bf16x2 loads for the input x. Wait, for the input, x[d] and x[d+half] are not contiguous (they're half_d elements apart). So I can't use bf16x2 for the pair read. But I can use bf16x2 for x[d] and x[d+1] (two consecutive d values) if I process 2 pairs per thread. Hmm, this is getting complicated. Let me try a different approach. Let me just go with the simplest CUDA kernel (1 pair per thread, scalar loads) and see if the CUDA C++ overhead is lower than Triton. The key advantage of CUDA C++ is lower kernel launch overhead and more efficient instruction scheduling. Actually, let me also try something else: increase the Triton tile size. Let me try BLOCK=1024 (twice the current size) and see if shape 2 improves. Let me try this quick experiment. Actually, let me try something even simpler first. Let me combine the q and k kernels into one Triton launch (processing both in one kernel). This eliminates one kernel launch. The idea: linearize over B * (Hq + Hkv) * T rows, and determine whether each row is q or k based on its position. ```python @triton.jit def _mrope_kernel( Q_PTR, K_PTR, Q_ROT_PTR, K_ROT_PTR, POS_T_PTR, POS_H_PTR, POS_W_PTR, COS_PTR, SIN_PTR, n_rows, total_q_rows, # B * Hq * T T_val, Hq_val, Hkv_val, D: tl.constexpr, HALF: tl.constexpr, S0: tl.constexpr, S1: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_D: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_T: tl.constexpr, BLOCK: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) base_row = pid * BLOCK_T offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK) d = offs % BLOCK_D t_in_tile = offs // BLOCK_D row = base_row + t_in_tile row_valid = row < n_rows valid = row_valid # Determine q or k is_q = row < total_q_rows H = tl.where(is_q, Hq_val, Hkv_val) local_row = tl.where(is_q, row, row - total_q_rows) # Decode (b, h, t) t_idx = local_row % T_val r = local_row // T_val h_idx = r % H b_idx = r // H # Position bt = b_idx * T_val + t_idx pt = tl.load(POS_T_PTR + bt, mask=row_valid, other=0) ph = tl.load(POS_H_PTR + bt, mask=row_valid, other=0) pw = tl.load(POS_W_PTR + bt, mask=row_valid, other=0) # ... (rest of the kernel) # For the load/store, select q or k pointer # In Triton, I can't easily select between two pointers with a vector condition # I'd need to compute both offsets and load/store separately ``` Hmm, the issue is that in Triton, I can't easily switch between two pointers (q vs k) based on a per-element condition. I'd need to use `tl.load` with the appropriate pointer for each element. Triton doesn't support pointer selection like CUDA's ternary operator on pointers. I'd need to compute both q and k offsets, load both, and then select based on `is_q`. But that doubles the memory traffic. Alternative: use separate load/store for q and k, with masks: ```python # Load from q where is_q, from k where not is_q x_q = tl.load(Q_PTR + x_q_offset, mask=valid & is_q, other=0.0) x_k = tl.load(K_PTR + x_k_offset, mask=valid & (~is_q), other=0.0) x0 = tl.where(is_q, x_q, x_k) ``` This loads from both q and k for each element, but only one load is valid (the other is masked out). The hardware should handle this efficiently (masked-out loads don't actually access memory). But actually, masked-out loads in Triton do access memory (they load 0 or the `other` value). Wait, no. The `mask` parameter prevents out-of-bounds access. For in-bounds but semantically unwanted loads, the hardware still issues the load but the result is discarded. Hmm, this is wasteful. Each element would issue 2 loads (one for q, one for k) even though only one is needed. Let me think of a better approach. What if I interleave q and k rows differently? Alternative row ordering: process all q rows first, then all k rows. This is what I'm already doing (separate launches). Combining them doesn't save much because the kernel launch overhead is negligible. OK, let me abandon the combined kernel idea and focus on optimizing each kernel separately. Let me try two things: 1. Increase the tile size in the Triton kernel (BLOCK=1024) 2. Try a CUDA C++ kernel with vectorized loads Let me try option 1 first since it's a quick change. Actually, wait. Let me also check: is the Triton kernel compilation overhead significant? The benchmark does 30 iterations. The first iteration includes JIT compilation. Let me check if the benchmark warms up. Actually, looking at the benchmark output, the first shape takes longer (due to JIT compilation), but subsequent shapes are faster (using cached compilations). The benchmark should be measuring steady-state performance. Let me check the time_fn implementation. Actually, I should focus on the optimization, not the measurement methodology. Let me try increasing the tile size. Let me modify the launch to use larger tiles: ```python block_t = 1024 // block_d # target 1024 elements per program ``` For D=128: block_t = 8 For D=64: block_t = 16 This doubles the tile size compared to the current version (512 elements per program). Let me try this. Oh wait, I also want to try another optimization: use the gather approach instead of 6-row loading to reduce memory traffic. Let me try both changes together. Actually, let me be methodical. Let me try one change at a time: 1. First: increase tile size (BLOCK=1024) 2. Then: switch to gather approach 3. Then: try CUDA C++ if still not fast enough Let me start with option 1. Actually, let me also try a different optimization that might help shape 2: process multiple pairs per thread. In Triton, I can have each thread handle 2 pairs by using a larger BLOCK_D: ```python BLOCK_D = 2 * D # each thread handles 2 consecutive d values ``` But this doesn't make sense for the rotation, which needs x[d] and x[d+D/2]. Each pair needs two values from different halves of D. Let me think about this differently. Instead of increasing BLOCK_D (which is tied to D), I can increase BLOCK_T (rows per program). This is what I'm already doing. OK, let me just try larger BLOCK_T and see the results. Wait, I realize I should also try the gather approach to reduce memory traffic. Let me try both optimizations together. Here's the updated kernel with gather approach and larger tiles: ```python @triton.jit def _mrope_kernel( X_PTR, OUT_PTR, POS_T_PTR, POS_H_PTR, POS_W_PTR, COS_PTR, SIN_PTR, n_rows, T_val, H_val, D: tl.constexpr, HALF: tl.constexpr, S0: tl.constexpr, S1: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_D: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_T: tl.constexpr, BLOCK: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) base_row = pid * BLOCK_T offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK) d = offs % BLOCK_D t_in_tile = offs // BLOCK_D row = base_row + t_in_tile row_valid = row < n_rows valid = row_valid t_idx = row % T_val r = row // T_val h_idx = r % H_val b_idx = r // H_val bt = b_idx * T_val + t_idx pt = tl.load(POS_T_PTR + bt, mask=row_valid, other=0) ph = tl.load(POS_H_PTR + bt, mask=row_valid, other=0) pw = tl.load(POS_W_PTR + bt, mask=row_valid, other=0) # Gather cos/sin (only load needed values) d_in_half = d % HALF pos = tl.where(d_in_half < S0, pt, tl.where(d_in_half < S0 + S1, ph, pw)) cos_val = tl.load(COS_PTR + pos * D + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) sin_val = tl.load(SIN_PTR + pos * D + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) # Input x_base = (b_idx * T_val + t_idx) * H_val * D + h_idx * D x0 = tl.load(X_PTR + x_base + d, mask=valid, other=0.0) partner_d = tl.where(d < HALF, d + HALF, d - HALF) x1 = tl.load(X_PTR + x_base + partner_d, mask=valid, other=0.0) sign = tl.where(d < HALF, -1.0, 1.0) out = x0 * cos_val + sign * x1 * sin_val out_base = (b_idx * H_val + h_idx) * T_val * D + t_idx * D tl.store(OUT_PTR + out_base + d, out, mask=valid) ``` And the launch: ```python block_t = 1024 // block_d # target 1024 elements per program block = block_t * block_d ``` Let me try this. Actually wait, there's a subtlety with the gather approach and tiled processing. The `pos * D + d` address uses `pos` which varies per row (different t_in_tile). So the address is a 2D gather: different rows and different d values. In Triton, this is handled as a vector gather. But the efficiency depends on the access pattern. Let me think about it. For a tile of BLOCK_T rows and D columns: - Row 0: pos = pt0 for d in [0, s0), ph0 for d in [s0, s0+s1), pw0 for d in [s0+s1, D) Wait, d ranges from 0 to D-1. d_in_half = d % HALF. For d < HALF: axis is based on d_in_half = d. For d >= HALF: d_in_half = d - HALF. So the access pattern is: - Row 0, d in [0, s0): address = pt0 * D + d (from temporal cache row) - Row 0, d in [s0, s0+s1): address = ph0 * D + d (from height cache row) - Row 0, d in [s0+s1, HALF): address = pw0 * D + d (from width cache row) - Row 0, d in [HALF, HALF+s0): address = pt0 * D + d (temporal, mirrored) - Row 0, d in [HALF+s0, HALF+s0+s1): address = ph0 * D + d (height, mirrored) - Row 0, d in [HALF+s0+s1, D): address = pw0 * D + d (width, mirrored) Within each segment, the addresses are contiguous. Across segments, the addresses jump to different cache rows. Within a segment of length s0 (or s1 or s2), the addresses are pt0*D + [segment_start, segment_end), which is contiguous. So the gather pattern for each row is 6 contiguous segments (3 in the first half, 3 in the second half, but the second half uses the same cache rows as the first half). Actually, the mirrored segments use the same cache rows but different d offsets. So the full pattern for one row accesses at most 3 unique cache rows, each at D consecutive offsets. Wait, no. The mirrored segments access the same cache row but at different d offsets. For example: - d in [0, s0): pt0 * D + [0, s0) -> contiguous from pt0's row - d in [HALF, HALF+s0): pt0 * D + [HALF, HALF+s0) -> contiguous from pt0's row These are two segments from the same cache row, but at different offsets. The first segment starts at pt0*D and the second at pt0*D+HALF. Since the cache row has D consecutive bf16 values, the two segments are: [0, s0) and [HALF, HALF+s0). These are not contiguous (there's a gap from s0 to HALF). So they're two separate contiguous loads from the same cache row. In total, per row, we have 6 contiguous segments from 3 cache rows: - pt0's row: [0, s0) and [HALF, HALF+s0) - ph0's row: [s0, s0+s1) and [HALF+s0, HALF+s0+s1) - pw0's row: [s0+s1, HALF) and [HALF+s0+s1, D) Each segment is s0, s1, or s2 elements long. The total elements loaded per row is 2*(s0+s1+s2) = 2*HALF = D. Which makes sense. So the gather loads exactly D cos values and D sin values per row. The memory access pattern is 6 small contiguous loads per row per (cos/sin). With BLOCK_T rows per tile, we have BLOCK_T * 6 * 2 = BLOCK_T * 12 contiguous load segments. For BLOCK_T=16: 192 segments. Each segment is small (s0=8, s1=12, s2=12 for D=64). The total data is BLOCK_T * D * 2 * 2 = 16 * 64 * 4 = 4096 bytes of cos/sin. With the 6-row approach, we load BLOCK_T * 6 * D * 2 = 16 * 6 * 64 * 2 = 12288 bytes of cos/sin. That's 3x more. For L2-cached data, the difference might not matter much. But for DRAM traffic, the gather approach is more efficient. However, the gather approach has more complex addressing, which might lead to more memory transactions (each small segment might be a separate transaction). With s0=8 bf16 = 16 bytes, this is 1/8 of a cache line (128 bytes). The hardware would still need to load the full cache line. So the actual memory traffic depends on whether the segments are in the same cache line. For the cache row of D=64 bf16 = 128 bytes, which is exactly 1 cache line. All segments from one cache row are in the same cache line. So the hardware loads the entire cache line once and serves all segments from it. With the 6-row approach, we load 6 cache lines per row (3 cos rows + 3 sin rows). With the gather approach, we also load from the same 6 cache lines (3 for cos, 3 for sin), but only access D values from each. So the actual cache line traffic is the same: 6 cache lines per row. The difference is only in the number of load instructions and the data volume returned to registers. With the 6-row approach: 6 load instructions, each loading D values = 6 * D = 384 values (for D=64). With the gather approach: the same 6 cache lines are accessed, but only D values are needed. However, Triton might still load the full cache line and then extract the needed values. Hmm, actually, the hardware loads cache lines, not individual values. If the 3 segments from one cache row are all within the same cache line (which they are for D=64 bf16 = 128 bytes = 1 cache line), then the hardware loads the cache line once and serves all requests from it. So the DRAM traffic is the same for both approaches: 6 cache lines per row. The difference is in register usage and instruction count: - 6-row: 6 large loads, many registers (6 * D values in flight) - gather: more smaller loads, fewer registers For a bandwidth-bound kernel, register pressure matters less. The 6-row approach might actually be better because it has fewer load instructions and better memory coalescing. OK, I've been going back and forth. Let me just try the larger tile size with the 6-row approach and see the results. If it's not good enough, I'll try the gather approach and then CUDA C++. Let me modify the solution to use BLOCK=1024. OK, let me code this up. Let me also try BLOCK=2048 to see if even larger tiles help.

+from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline
+
+_CUDA_SRC = r"""
+#include <torch/extension.h>
+#include <cuda.h>
+#include <cuda_bf16.h>
+#include <c10/cuda/CUDAStream.h>
+
+// Each block: one (b,t), H_TILE heads, half_d pairs per head.
+// Threads: (half_d, H_TILE) 2-D block.
+// Shared: 6 * D bf16 values (cos/sin for 3 axes).
+template <int HALF_D, int H_TILE, int S0, int S1>
+__global__ void __launch_bounds__(512)
+mrope_kernel(
+    __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ out,        // (B, H_out, T, D)
+    const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ x,    // (B, T, H_out, D)
+    const int64_t* __restrict__ pos_t,
+    const int64_t* __restrict__ pos_h,
+    const int64_t* __restrict__ pos_w,
+    const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ cos_cache,
+    const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ sin_cache,
+    const int B, const int T, const int H, const int D,
+    const int n_groups          // B * T
+) {
+    // Shared memory: 6 rows of D bf16
+    // Layout: cos_t[0..D-1], sin_t[0..D-1], cos_h, sin_h, cos_w, sin_w
+    __shared__ __nv_bfloat16 smem[6 * 128];  // max D=128
+
+    const int half_d = HALF_D;
+    const int tx = threadIdx.x;   // pair index  0..half_d-1
+    const int ty = threadIdx.y;   // head index  0..H_TILE-1
+    const int tid = ty * half_d + tx;
+    const int n_threads = half_d * H_TILE;
+
+    // Block mapping: blockIdx.x encodes (group, head_tile)
+    const int n_head_tiles = (H + H_TILE - 1) / H_TILE;
+    const int group_id = blockIdx.x / n_head_tiles;
+    const int head_tile = blockIdx.x % n_head_tiles;
+
+    if (group_id >= n_groups) return;
+
+    const int b = group_id / T;
+    const int t = group_id % T;
+    const int h_start = head_tile * H_TILE;
